B004ZNA5JO|3.0|Let's face it: After The Wall, Pink Floyd as a unit were never the same; one could even argue they weren't the same after Wish You Were Here, and the Final Cut, to me, will always be considered a Roger Waters record with two members of Pink Floyd being present. After much wrangling through lawyers, contracts, courts and countersuits, Gilmour and Mason were granted permission and awarded the right to continue as Pink Floyd confirming once and for all, that Waters had simply exited the group(even though his ego may have alluded otherwise). And so, with Bob Ezrin in tow with co-production and also instrumental aid, David Gilmour cultivates HIS vision of moving forward with 1987's A Momentary Lapse of Reason. Learning to Fly would be culled as the single and is definable and successful as an updated fascimile of late 80's era Floyd. The first half of the record shows Gilmour writing with collaborators such as Ezrin, session Keysman Jon Carin, Anthony Moore and Pat Leonard including another definable and beautiful gem in On the Turning Away. Dogs of War has a tendency to be a bit &#34;by numbers&#34; here and smells of self-parody while One Slip is a strong track but serves better as a solo piece that would've fit well on Gilmour's prior About Face record.  The second half of Lapse is really where the appeal of this record lies, starting with Yet Another Movie and on through an instrumental Round and Around, these tracks consolidate and give relevance to this current aggregation led by Gilmour, despite the number of session player credits(albeit some very talented ones, i.e. Tony Levin, Carmine Appice, Tom Scott and Bill Payne to name a few). Then begins an even more Floydian flavor with The New Machine segments split into 2 parts with the blissful and tuneful Terminal Frost instrumental as its centerpiece. This track is drenched in jazz/rock and is driven by the soprano and tenor sax arrangements by Tom Scott and Scott Page respectively. Despite the fact that the albumdoesn't properly give track by track personnel credits, thereby making one wonder how true of a comeback release it is, Ezrin's production and Gilmour's arrangement values are impeccably contemporary, innovative and seamlessly cohesive enough to see this recording through, and ultimately Momentary Lapse is a success, for the most part. The record closes with Sorrow which has a bit of darkness about itself, but is a most strong closer to a record that offers up Gilmour's vision of Pink Floyd mach III in a most convincing manner. I give Lapse 3 and a half stars because while I feel the musicianship is very good and professionally played, along with a strong production and arrangement value as its other solid point, its still not a true Floyd record without the involvement of Roger Waters. Just as I had criticized the Final Cut in not having Wright or Gilmour involved within the songwriting process of that record, the same applies to this one from the other side of the coin, as it were. Although Wright appears in the credits to this album, it wouldn't be confirmed of his actual studio contributions to this phase of the group until 94's Division Bell, which were much more realized and complete there. 3 and a half Stars.

B004OC07N0|4.0|I liked the focus on the first 90 days in a new managerial role: tips for building credibility quickly, and tips for embarking on fixing what needs fixing while preserving what needs preserving. I liked the idea of breaking the first 90 days into 30-day chunks, with subplans for each stage. I liked the STaRS model as a way to characterize the situation: Startups where the whole group is creating something new; Turnarounds where you've got a fixer-upper and they know it; Realignments where they need some course corrections but not everyone knows or agrees; and Sustaining success where they're doing well and need to maintain it and grow. It was a useful model for comparing the likely challenges, opportunities, and areas of focus. I liked the insight that the STaRS model can apply at multiple levels: from a single project to a divisional unit to the whole company.<br /><br />One aspect of the STaRS model struck me as needing elaboration. Different STaRS situations were, in part, characterized by whether the people involved agreed on how well things were going and what direction to take. That seemed unrealistically oversimplified. There'll often be people who are on board and those who aren't. The question is who. Maybe senior management has a consensus, but your staff doesn't. Maybe your staff has a consenus, but it's not the same as management's, and then your key customers have yet another view. Sometimes you've got overall consensus in one group or another, but one or two key people within the group see the situation differently. With each of the four situations in the STaRS model, it should be important to know where you have agreement on status and directions, and where you don't.<br /><br />The book had its weak spots. Sometimes, the advice was overly simplistic, to the point that it felt like padding. I should set realistic goals? I should ask good questions? No kidding, really? I wouldn't think that anyone in a new managerial role would consciously plan to setbad goals and ask bad questions. (Their goals and questions might turn out to be bad, but they didn't go into the situation wanting to do the job poorly.) Also, the author's advice sometimes strayed quite far from business. Talk to me about the different challenges facing startups and turnarounds, sure, but I don't think he's qualified to tell me how to maintain a work-life balance (Chapter 9).<br /><br />I had hoped the checklists at the end of each chapter would offer practical steps toward creating and implementing a 90-day plan, including good tips on when to do various things. Instead, the checklist items were rather nebulous and non-directive. The \\"checklist\\" label was a misnomer, compared to what I normally expect from a checklist. With each new chapter, I spent less and less time reading the checklist.<br /><br />The anecdotes that started each chapter didn't do anything for me. Every one of them fell into one of two forms: a) Jane Doe succeeded by doing what I'm about to tell you to do, or b) Jane Doe failed by not doing what I'm about to tell you to do. They felt like simplistic self-justification, not insightful explorations of the advice being offered.<br /><br />I would have liked some summary comparisons, for later reference and reminder, of the different STaRS situations, and a summary structure for the elements of the 90-day plan. This is all stuff that was strewn throughout the book, but some reference summaries would have been helpful.<br /><br />All in all, though, there was good advice in the book, especially in comparing different aspects of the STaRS model. The book is suitable for anyone in a new leadership role. I don't see why some people are saying this is for senior leadership only. A new front-line supervisor could benefit from a 90-day plan too.<br /><br />The Kindle version worked pretty well. I saw that some older reviews of the Kindle version complained about badly formatted tables and so on. That appears to have been resolved. The tables and figures are intact. However, you can't resize them and they don't use your chosen text size, so you might find them hard to read. The table of contents was pretty good, covering the chapters and the front and end matter, but not the subsections within chapters.

B000I0QKDS|5.0|First I want to say this is a really classic collaboration between Beck and Stewart and every track is great. I will resist the temptation to say neither man has done anything better since. I love Jeff's later fusion stuff (for example, check out What Mama Said with Jennifer Batten, Rollin and Tumblin with Imogene Heap, or Cause We've Ended as Lovers with in the group with Tal Wilkenfeld) and of course Rod went on to do a lot of great stuff where a flashy guitarist would have been a distraction. But the collaboration here works perfectly. Unfortunately Rod was too good to be in somebody else's group for long.<br /><br />I want to take issue with the fellow who criticized the inclusion of Ole Man River. I had this album as a kid and considered Ole Man River one of the highlights. It's a perfect vehicle for Stewart, who loves to ham it up on these slow spiritual-like numbers (check out their glorious cover of Curtis Mayfield's 'People Get Ready' in a similar vein.) It's a perfect vehicle for Beck too, because the song gives him plenty of holes to fill. Interweaving with Rod's hoarse-voiced, melodramatic posturing, Jeff sets off roman candle riffs bursting with melody and whiplash electronic flourishes while the tympani thunder away in the background. It's just magnificent and if you don't like it what are you doing listening to Rod Stewart? The man is basically Broadway from tip to toe. And there's no shortage of theatricality in Jeff's betcha-never-heard-THIS-before guitar work either. This disc is not for Robert Johnson fans, at least not when they're (we're) in a somber, worshipful mood. It's for people who think playing and listening to blues should be FUN! - a politically incorrect thought. It's no accident that Beck went on to collaborate with members of Vanilla Fudge, a band known for incredibly bombastic covers of other people's two-minute songs, which sounded fabulous but were too over the top to be taken seriously by critics. I think Jeff was drawn to the Fudge by their deliberate excessiveness and opulent sound. Rod and Jeff are a dynamite combo but there's a playfulness, sometimes bordering on self-parody, in their work, which is as it should be. If you believe in sound for sound's sake and don't care whether they lyrics contain profound insights into the human condition, you're the right audience.

0439023521|4.0|The Hunger Games has some flaws, many of which are pointed out in these reader reviews.  I found it difficult to follow the first person, present tense narrative for the first 50 pages before my mind and eyes settled in on that writing style.  It has been accused of plagiarizing Battle Royale and Collins' character development skills have come under question.  The totalitarian society lacks definition and there are some amazing twists that solve pressing plot problems.  All this is true, however...<br /><br />Not every book needs to be perfectly done.  Not all books define characters in a satisfying way and most books take thoughts and ideas from other works.  To define a \\"good read\\" one only has to answer the question \\"does it take you away from the day-to-day for a few hours and interest you in a different world?\\"<br /><br />Good books provide an escape and maintain a level of anticipation that keeps the reader wondering what comes next.  How will our protagonists get out of this mess?  On these accounts, The Hunger Games is a good book and I'm confident that the second and third books will have the same positive attributes.<br /><br />It has the additional benefit of being wildly popular with young readers.  Middle and high school students have embraced and devoured these books in unprecedented numbers.  Before the admonishments on the brutality and cruelty exhibited in The Hunger Games, I'm sure our teens have seen much worse on cable tv and video games.  The fact that it has attracted a veritable army of teenage \\"readers\\" only bodes well for the future literacy of our young ones.  Sure, its not Shakespeare, but reading anything at this age is way better than reading nothing at all and it may just light a spark of interest which someday may become a raging fire.<br /><br />If you doubt me, read it yourself.  It's worth the price.  And while you're at it, buy one for your teenage child, niece, nephew, grandkid or neighbor.  That is, if they haven't already read it.<br /><br />John E. 1476712042|5.0|I was so excited to get my hands on an advanced reader of Beautiful Disaster, as I had heard so many different thoughts on the book. I wanted to read the book so that I could form my own opinion of the story. I have to say, this book is a roller-coaster ride, and will leave you with very mixed emotions.<br /><br />Abby Abernathy has a secret past, one that she has run a long way from. A new school with new friends, a new beginning is exactly what she needs. All is well, until she catches the attention of the school bad boy, Travis Maddox, who is completely blown away by her resistance to him.  Never one to be rejected, Travis does whatever he can to get Abby into his life. But can Abby live with the dominating Travis in her life?<br /><br />Abby, the main character, was both headstrong and also extremely frustrating. I love that she stood up to Travis's crazy ways, for the most part, especially when there were red flags going up all over the place. However, it was insanely frustrating that she used other guys just to hurt Travis. It really made me want to shake her and tell her to quit playing games. But other than that, I found her to be a likeable character.<br /><br />The thing I love most in male characters is the tortured hero. I don't know what it is, but I can't get enough of them. Travis is definitely a tortured soul.  My heart broke for him multiple times, but I also found him infuriating at times as well. He was such a completely complex character; I still haven't completely figured him out. Yes, he was overbearing and quite dominant, but he also had a completely different side to him as well, and that's the side that I fell in love with. He broke my heart more than a few times with his emotions. I love this man.  (On a side note; I totally picture Travis as Channing Tatum! Check out his movie Fighter, and you will see why.)<br /><br />The secondary characters were interesting as well, with my favorites being Shepley, Travis's cousin and America, who was Abby's best friend. America was a really great friend to Abby. There was also Finch, who Abby and America met on their first day of school, who was a loner just like Abby. I love him!<br /><br />If you love a good love story, full of emotional angst and drama, this is definitely the book for you. I loved the storyline as well. That kept me hooked, and flying through the pages faster than I expected to.<br /><br />There were a few small issues that I had with the book, however. As an adult that knows the difference between right and wrong, I found this book to be a fascinating read. As a mom to a teenage girl, I have many issues with the book. I can't imagine a teenager reading this and understanding how many things were wrong with relationship. For that one reason, I wouldn't recommend this for the younger YA crowd. (I had this problem with Twilight as well. While the story is romantic and understandable by most, younger girls that are easily swayed may not see the problems with the relationship; the possessiveness, the codependency, etc.) This book also contains a great deal of violence, sex, underage drinking, and vulgar language.  As an adult, this doesn't make me love the book any less, but as a parent, it makes me cautious with my extremely sheltered child. (That's just me though!)<br /><br />Overall, I really enjoyed this book, so much more than I expected to. This is a book that will stick with you long after you've read the last page. I can't wait for my finished copy to arrive so that I can read it again!  Also, we will also be getting a book in 2013 that is Beautiful Disaster from Travis's perspective; Walking Disaster. 0061452068|5.0|Three Views of the Future<br /><br />In general, worldwide has man's quality of life been improving or worsening in the last 100 years, or the last 20 years?  And is the future a bright or troubled one?  Many who bother themselves with such concerns  see a threatening future; there is concern about environmental damage,  unsustainable consumption of resources, and institutional failure.  In the latter case the nature of the perceived failure depends on an individual's ideological inclinations.  Matt Ridley (R) in his book,  \\"The Rational Optimist\\",  Thomas Homer-Dixon (H-D), his book- \\"The Upside of Down\\". and Richard Heinberg (H), in \\"The End of Growth\\",  provide three different scenarios for mankind's future.  Ridley' s  optimistic assessment of the future is based on mankind's past success, the overall upward trend in man's quality of life, including especially the recent dramatic improvements due to technological innovations. In contrast, Homer-Dixon and Heinberg imagine a more difficult future which will require substantial changes in the way we manage our affairs, but they have different emphases and scope.  H-D's view is through the prism of societies' occasional failures.  H-D. especially and H. in some particulars are more concerned with institutional structures and operations of societies: government, business, financial, etc. while R emphasizes progress in technology, medicine agriculture, etc.  Heinberg predicts  a dramatic change in the trajectory of the world's economies due to increasing cost of exploiting  the world's  natural resources.<br /><br />Ridley's book's subtitle frames his narrative: \\"How Prosperity Evolved\\". . He argues that the history of cultural and technology innovation that led to the remarkable achievements of the past 200,000 years  provides reason to expect a continued upward trending path despite the incidence of incompetent governance, dysfunctional institutions, and bureaucracy, both public and private.  An example of  his supporting evidence: between 1955 and 2000 wealth per capita tripled worldwide.  Ridley's prescription for future success-maximize the opportunity for innovation and promote growth which is necessary for the improved welfare for all. The important thing for Ridley is that barriers to innovation be minimized and that this will be accomplished by encouraging the creativity of individuals and entrepreneurs acting particularly in small and flexible organizations and freeing up markets to facilitate such activities.<br /><br />Thomas Homer-Dixon's approach in \\"The Upside of Down\\"  uses a historical perspective to examine the mechanisms that lead to societal failure illustrated for instance by the Roman empire and he draws parallels with those situations and now.  More than the other two authors he pays attention to the operation of societies'  institutions, their decision making strategies and mechanisms for coping with challenges  Although he views future challenges as daunting, as can be seen from his title, he doesn't foresee inevitable decline, rather he sees the challenges as opportunities whose confrontation can lead to new heights of achievement for mankind.  Homer-Dixon along with Ridley stresses the need to promote innovation but H-D's major concern is institutional structures while Ridley's emphasis is on technological development  H-D sees the need for more collective action but not necessarily government action which often serves the desires of economic elites.  For H-D a basic problem is that institutions naturally would rather deal with failures incrementally than attempt fundamental reform and restructuring.  Generally the coping process involves denial first  and then when a situation is sufficiently threatening incremental repairs are attempted including added bureaucracy.  Society's cultural structures: bureaucracy, urban systems, the military, economic arrangements, and the legal system tend to grow more complex.  The result is prolongation of the tried and true  with the expectation of minimizing harm in the short term usually while protecting the interests of the powerful.  The problem is<br />that such development is accompanied by increasing rigidity, instability, and inefficiency.  Often managers and \\"experts\\"  are poor at prediction and fail because they don't understand the complex, dfficult-to-manage systems that they've  created.  Our tax and financial systems, and the bureaucratic response to 9/11 are examples that come to mind. Such behavior is characteristic of all of man's institutions. Homer-Dixon relates this process to the thermodynamic concept of entropy- the more complex the system the more energy required to maintain it's organization.<br /><br />Heinberg focuses more narrowly on two topics:  the first,  economic and financial policy and performance and the second,  the impact on the economy of growing constraints on  exploitation of natural resources-especially energy. For Heinberg (as for Ridley) the foundation for the spectacular improvements of the last 150 years is consumption of cheap-to-reclaim but ultimately limited nonrenewables. But fossil fuel production cannot continue to Increase indefinitely.  The cost of exploitation- the \\"low hanging fruit\\" or \\"diminishing return\\" concept applies to fossil fuels, valuable ores, water, land, etc. which experience increasing marginal costs of exploitation as they are depleted.  For instance, deep-ocean oil is more expensive than oil produced on dry land.  The concept of \\"Peak Oil\\" -when discovery of new fields no longer keeps up with the increasing consumption driven by economic development and an increasing population (and declining per capita share) derives from this reality. For renewables, the rate of replenishment controls the consumption rate  And while substitution of nonrenewables helps with the supply issue,  its also true that  they're generally more costly and they have their own substantial environmental impacts. Given this reality he argues that  there will be increasing downward force on economic growth.<br /><br />A second harmful factor is the proliferation of negative environmental impacts. As do many others he sees increasing future risk caused by population growth for at least the next 60 years, citing the environmental destruction seen, for instance, in the Amazon basin and S.E. Asia. He argues that it will be difficult for financial systems to adjust to reduced resource availability and increasing environmental real costs.  Again this will be a drag on economic growth.<br /><br />Like H-D but less broadly he considers political and financial factors which are not central in Ridley's narrative.  Regarding economics he sees our future put at risk because of the accumulation of unsustainable levels of debt.  This is the result of the failure of individuals, their political representatives, and business organizations to concern themselves with  long-term consequences and fiscal integrity.  He doesn't see cause for optimism given voter's incapacity to deal with the increasingly complex issues they confront, the role of media in reducing the quality of civil discourse, and the corruption endemic in systems where those possessing great wealth may use it to advance their own interests at the expense of the rest.<br /><br />Ridley stresses that he is not a Panglossian and doesn't ignore the \\"bumpy\\" nature of the upward slope. Referring to current political and economic issues, he refers to  \\"rent-seekers and influence-peddlers\\" and the need to combat corruption and inefficiency, damning both corporate and  government disfunction.  He is clear when extolling the efficiencies of markets that they are useful for providing goods and services but that unregulated markets for financial assets are dangerous.  He remarks on the danger resulting from the increasingly  integrated nature of the world which makes possible the impact of \\"foolish  ideas\\" on large populations.  It's clear he doesn't fit into the right-wing ideological slots generally associated with his optimistic outlook.<br /><br />He refers specifically to the time between 1100 and 1150 when three great nation states simultaneously followed reactionary paths and discouraged innovation that led to long term<br />decline. The Ming Dynasty pursued retrograde policies for several hundred years that put China on a downward trajectory and Mao's policies extended that trend.  But  there remained enclaves that didn't follow along and progress resumed.  A recent example was the US action after W.W.II when it had the foresight and resources to promote rebuilding in Asia and Europe.  He asks what would have happened had all  succumbed to reaction.  He doubts that a large-scale or universal catastrophe could happen now; he imagines that even if Europe or the US falters there will remain progressive islands in the sea of decline that will find ways forward.  He suggests that perhaps China, India, Brazil, or some other could be that island.  His conclusion: regardless of setbacks, by almost any measure our trajectory in general through time has been upward, that although the doom sayers have always been fashionable at least as far back as recorded history, in fact their predictions of final decline clearly have been wrong.<br /><br />Ridley argues that progress requires creative destruction;  culling the old and outdated while creating the new and improved.  He refers to \\"catallaxy\\"- a term coined by Hyak signifying the production of spontaneous order from the flux of change. There is a chapter in his book- \\"Catallaxy\\" in which he describes future changes that he views favorably.  For instance he predicts that large corporations, political parties and government bureaucracies will \\"crumble and fragment\\".  Small is better and more dynamic with more fluid aggregations of people .  H-D has a similar view regarding the effectiveness of relatively informal bottom-up decision-making and initiative.<br /><br />How can we choose between these narratives; or to what extent can one or another be discounted?  All three are plausible and the authors' data and interpretations are persuasive for the most part. They share many of the same concerns,  acknowledging the challenges we face: the corruption of society's institutions, political and economic dysfunction, and the need to resolve the problems of the poor and the third world.  There is no explicit contradiction between Homer-Dixon's and Ridley's positions given their different starting points- the one concerned with specific failures, the decline of Rome, the initiation of W.W.I and the resulting Nazi and Bolshevik catastrophes, in contrast to the other's focus on the overall direction of mankind's progress.  H-D views history at higher resolution and despite the general  trend, all nation-states ultimately fail.<br /><br />H-D focuses attention on governance and decision-making. and H. and H-D  see danger arising from financial and political dysfunction.  R. implicitly assumes that institutional failures that are of particular concern for H-D will be remedied by future innovators, that resolution of such problems can be expected because until now mankind  has overcome them.  But regarding man's future in the political and economic context,  the historical record is less supportive of faith in the upward trend in all but the very long term.  By making the achievements following the renaissance- the scientific and technological revolution- medicine,  agriculture,  and manufacture the major focus of his narrative,  R. has picked aspects of man's culture that would most likely feature successful behaviors; achievement should be easier in these arenas for even modestly functional societies.  H-D and Heinberg undoubtedly would agree with most of R's narrative regarding technological progress.  As for Heinberg he sees a future for which history provides little precedent because of increasing restrictions on resource supply. Thus there remains the major contradiction between Ridley's view on one side and Homer-Dixon and Heinberg's view on the other: namely the desirability and possibility of future robust economic growth.  Ridley asserts that  future economic growth is not only possible but is a moral imperative given that it is a necessary condition for continued improvement in the human condition, particularly for developing countries and the poor everywhere.<br /><br />All three authors are unpersuasive in some particulars.  From my perspective some of R's enthusiasms should be characterized as Panglossian. One quote: \\"Politicians are increasingly corks tossed on waves of public opinion\\" which he finds encouraging.  His view of voter sophistication is unsupported by most of the research of which I'm aware.  He extolls the advantages of people assembling their own news sources.  Will this further enable confirmation bias and herd-mentality?  Electronic media, the internet, etc.  exercise increasing political influence.  Has the result been better informed AND more analytic citizens employing critical thinking skills, or are we now more subject to manipulation, group think, and increasingly enabled to find support for cherished beliefs untested by exposure to alternative views encouraging comparison between one's own reality and others'?  H-D  addresses this concern.  There is increasing distrust and a rejection of many of our institutions- governmental and others including the scientific establishment.  I imagine that , one thing more counterproductive for citizens and decision-makers than uncritically accepting views of experts is to uncritically reject them.  Another difficulty with R's narrative is his focus on aggregate wealth and failure to credit wealth distribution trends.  One environmental impact he discounts is ocean acidification and resulting risk to marine biosystems, maintaining that ocean water is highly buffered and whatever the impact, the results should be mixed.  It's fair to say that most workers in this field don't agree with his ideas.<br /><br />Heinberg's  arguments concerning economic policy are compelling but his prognostication concerning resources- Peak Oil, etc. is less so; resource constraints must assuredly have an increasing impact, but the time line for such developments seems uncertain.  He doesn't convince me of it being crucial in next 20-40 years especially with technological advances in transportation, conservation, developments with renewable energy including nuclear, and extraction of natural gas.  His arguments are an example of the \\"now syndrome\\" -whatever the problem, now is \\"the turning point\\", coincidental with a book's publication.  For one glimpse of a less restricted future go the the web site:  \\"passiv haus\\".  Another difficulty for me:  he sees a return to a kind of version of the past; where more people will  sew and sow- be involved in producing the food and other goods that they require. as was the condition before the industrial age.  This is certainly counter to the view of progress as seen by Ridley.  While such basic activities should be pursued by those who find them rewarding,  hopefully there will be increasing possibility to choose one's life/work style;  that there will be even more specialization with more choices to create and recreate.  Hopefully  educational resources will be increasingly utilized to allow for personal achievement on and off the job.<br /><br />There are two issues barely noted by the three authors. They all apparently favor free markets, but given the centrality of profit and related  labor  cost,  there is an inherent conflict between the aim of maximizing citizens' economic welfare and opportunity for rewarding employment and maximizing return on capital.  Perhaps the last two hundred years has been featuring a singularity- the scarcity of skilled  labor relative to demand.  Developments in electronics and automation will result in increasing productivity in many lines of work; assuredly there will still be rewarding engagement in agriculture, resource capture and reprocessing, many service sectors- medicine, food service and sales, and  R & D,  but overall,the trend is a decreasing labor input.  What is the expectation for the size of workers'  piece of the economic pie?  The challenge will be to decouple work from maintenance of a satisfactory living standard;  our economy will only function at a high level if there are economically capable consumers.  Obviously this is a moral issue as well as one simply of supply and demand.  An observer, especially a wage-earner living through the years 1938-1970 might have a different view of inevitability of economic progress than one living through the time from the 70s until now.<br /><br />Everything considered, I believe that all three authors provide a useful bases for thinking  about our future and what we can do to make it a more rewarding one.  Heinberg's no- economic-growth scenario certainly impliess a radically new course for mankind.  For those who are inclined to a philosophical mindset  Ridley will be encouraging.  Then again if you were a small landowner in the 30s Russia,  or a Roman citizen in the year 400,  or an American laborer today you might buy Ridley's premise but nevertheless feel more doubtful about your future and \\"the future\\".  It' seems to me that H-D's analysis might be more useful for decision makers who are looking for tools to formulate possible paths into the future.

With Gary Rossington being the only \\"pre crash\\" member left, it just doesn't feel the same. If you really want to listen to \\"new\\" Skynyrd, stick with \\"Vicious Cycle\\". It is a far superior album.

030758836X|2.0|Gone Girl was recommended to me by the clerk at a local bookstore, who saw me stocking up on Stephen King and Philip K. Dick and vehemently insisted that if I like \\"those kinds\\" of novels, I would love this one. She really talked it up -- the intriguing mystery, the sociopath character study, the shocking twists and turns; she even went so far as to call it \\"the most f___d up book\\" she had read in a long time. Sold.<br /><br />Listening to her was my first mistake. I had just finished reading King's Misery, one of the books I picked up at the same time (now there's a real sociopath character study), and I couldn't wait to get started on this one. Finishing the book, despite my instincts, was the second mistake. It was then that I accepted that I had been had, both by the clerk and the author. The story goes like this: Nick and Amy Dunne's marriage is on the rocks. From finances to romance, there's trouble everywhere. On the morning of their fifth anniversary, Amy goes missing, and signs point to Nick. As the police and public are closing in, Nick insists he is innocent, but who are we to believe, and look out for that plot twist! and blah... blah... blah.<br /><br />Cliche to be sure, but at the risk of sounding bitter, let me just say that the first part of the novel starts magnificently -- so much so that it's enough to believe, to hope, to pray that you won't be let down. The first part focuses on Nick and the peeling away of his rotten layers as the conflict fully develops. In this first section, the characters are fascinating, the imagery is rich (the taste of bile in Nick's throat, the sun pointing an accusatory ray-finger), and the story is believable. During part one, Flynn succeeds at getting the reader to accept what she wants them to, and it makes for an engrossing start. Thank you, savvy bookstore clerk!<br /><br />And then comes part two. And three. And this is where the entire thing falls apart. The old bait and switcheroo. Reading Gone Girl is akin towatching some blockbuster movie that all your dim-witted neighbors just adored, only to find yourself sitting in the theater feeling completely cheated. By the second half of the book, everything that made the first half great has now grown tired -- the characters and their preposterous actions are more and more banal, the imagery takes a backseat to boring dialogue, and the plot development becomes less believable. It's not a page-turner. It's not gripping to the point that you can't put it down. You only want to finish to just see if you're right, that it will indeed end disappointingly. And despite the hopeful start, Flynn backs herself into a corner and is unable to write herself out, leaving the reader with a ridiculous, predictable, and unsatisfying ending that leaves much to be desired. And believe me, I \\"get\\" the ending -- I get the greater comment it's making, I get the absurdity of it all, I get the point, I get that there could be a sequel, I get it; it's just glaringly obvious and terribly boring.<br /><br />So, a great start, moments of clarity, but overall truly disappointing. Typical bestseller rubbish. Like my movie analogy earlier, I have no doubt that there are plenty of bored, obtuse people that will find Gone Girl absolutely thrilling -- I can imagine a number of people in my life who will think this is the greatest drama ever played out on the page -- but then again, people like a lot of stuff. Like monster trucks. Or Applebees.<br /><br />And to think, that same clerk had the audacity to say, \\"And if you like this -- be sure to check out Dostoyevsky!\\" Uh, yeah, thanks.

Her husband attempts to kill them both by crashing his plane; only Catherine survives to die in a cave while her lover is gone in search of help.<br /><br />In few words, Fiennes if elegant and desperate as Count Almasy, and Kristen Scott Thomas as Catherine is at first elegant and cool, then wonderfully passionate. B001QB9H1A|4.0|No one should ever go to a Luc Besson movie expecting brain power. But for anyone who wants a good action fix, his movies always deliver. \\"Transporter 3\\", the third installment of the Besson-penned \\"Transporter\\" series starring Jason Staham, not only provides the fix but also a suggestion that one should leave their brain out the front door for a piece of the fun.<br /><br />The first two \\"Transporter\\" movies were run-of-the-mill action movies that had some impressive fighting sequences but generic storytelling. \\"3\\" is easily the most inventive and enjoyable due to the fact that it takes implausibility to a whole new level and is aware of it. For example, how to make of the incomprehensible plot where the sharp-suited baddie (played with irresistible oiliness by Robert Knepper) kidnaps a Ukrainian minister's daughter named Valentina (Natalya Rudakova) to persuade him to bring eight toxic container ships into the Odessa harbor? Even if he did, what makes one think that he can get away with it, with not only so many ships but so much substance lingering on board? For some reason, this involves the Transporter (Jason Staham), who is blackmailed into traveling halfway across Europe with Valentina until the baddie can succeed in persuading the minister (why they can't just have her locked up in a basement or a building instead of hiring an experienced killer to travel across Europe I have no idea?). But here's the twist: Staham and the girl are forced to wear bracelets that will explode if they walk past 75 feet from the car.<br /><br />Yes, as you can see, the plot makes no sense. But who cares about the plot when you want some action? The incomprehensible plot is all just an excuse to see Staham kick some a--, drive cars at 200 mph and get lovey-dovey with the Ukrainian hostage. The car chases are entertaining enough (particularly the one where Staham has to drive in an angular position to avoid getting sandwiched by two trucks) but the explosive bracelet element adds greater pleasure (and silliness) to the fun. One of my favorite scenes that would never happen in real life is where Staham is fired from his driving service and is forced to ride a conveniently placed bicycle to catch up to the car before he explodes. Then in a really great scene that has to be seen to be believed, Staham and his car take a plunge in the water. To avoid drowning AND blowing up, Staham takes out two rafts from the trunk and fills them up using the tires' air to cause the car to ascend to the surface. Moments later, the car works fine and he drives off after the baddie.<br /><br />To accentuate the pacing and make the action more exciting than it is, Besson and director Oliver Megaton rely on fast-cutting, unconventional angles, flashbacks, dissolves and whirling camera movements to create a kaleidoscope of visual eye-candies that are pleasing, even if implausibility is obvious. In an early scene, when Staham checks on a transporter after crashing through his house, the mere mention of \\"Malcolm\\" causes the movie to flashback to the scene where Staham rejects a job by Knepper's accomplices, leading to a frantic melee. Besson may not be a smart storyteller but he and his crew are gifted in using visuals and editing to take a potentially disastrous product and turn it into a lean, mean fighting machine.<br /><br />The characterization, as usual in a Besson movie, is two-dimensional but serves its purpose. Knepper makes a nasty villain; Rudakova looks good, even when she mangles English; and Jason Staham is a cool, reserved badass, never laughing but always kicking butt. Franois Berl&eacute;and, who plays Staham's ally Inspector Tarconi, provides appropriate comic relief and support for our main hero.<br /><br />There are moments in the movie that leave you wondering how that's even possible but \\"Transporter 3\\" doesn't care what you think; it just wants to entertain. In that sense, it does its job. Sure, it's mindless fun but it's mindless fun done with style.<br /><br />Recommended.e.<br /><br />Recommended.

B0017O1MIM|5.0|Praise is often lavished too readily but for \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\" all the accolades are justified. This film owes its place among cinema's greatest achievements because of a quality which occasionally emerges when talent combines to create something special.<br /><br />It's impossible not to admire the grandeur of the sweeping desert scenes, photographed by Freddie Young in Super Panavision 70 (no CGI here!), blending with Maurice Jarre's majestic soundtrack; the imaginative transitional editing which so inspired the likes of Steven Spielberg; Robert Bolt's provocative screenplay with its ironic twists and character complexities; the spectacular battle sequences full of swirling colour; and not least the mesmerising Peter O'Toole as Lawrence, superbly supported by Omar Sharif, Anthony Quinn, Jack Hawkins and the rest. All these elements were moulded under the hands of director David Lean into a film that has become an enduring masterpiece. His vision, understanding of the subject matter, mastery of technique, eye for detail and uncompromising standards present us with a riveting desert saga based on the exploits of enigmatic British army officer, T.E. Lawrence, during the Arab Revolt in the First World War. Lean's work is now restored to its original pristine state, the transfer to blu-ray injecting it with a refreshing vitality.<br /><br />Some find it difficult to accept western actors in Arab roles and the film is indeed a product of the early 'sixties, when such things were common practice. But it should be remembered that Alec Guinness as Faisal was mistaken for the Prince on the set by people who knew him, and that Lean himself did not recognize Anthony Quinn when he first arrived made up in Arab costume. To my mind both actors appear perfectly natural alongside Omar Sharif, an ethnic Arab, in their scenes together. O'Toole's portrayal of Lawrence has drawn criticism also as too egotistical. The truth is that T.E. Lawrence was a difficult man to understand and that cinema by its nature must distort to some degree in order to create the illusion of reality. Where the film unquestionably succeeds is in its depiction of a character with divided loyalties struggling to reconcile personal demons amid the horrors of war. Although the real Lawrence comes across as a more subdued personality in his book \\"Seven Pillars of Wisdom\\"--which Bolt studied for his script--it's clear that he was a tortured soul.<br /><br />Such issues are, anyway, superfluous because this is not an historical documentary. It is an epic adventure story staged in a landscape of austere and perilous beauty. For newcomers to the film I should do no better than to quote the Lawrence character: \\"It's going to be fun.\\" To say they don't make films like this any more may be a cliche but in the case of \\"Lawrence of Arabia\\" they really don't.\\"that cinema by its nature must distort to some degree in order to create the illusion of reality. Where the film unquestionably succeeds is in its depiction of a character with divided loyalties struggling to reconcile personal demons amid the horrors of war. Although the real Lawrence comes across as a more subdued personality in his book \\"Seven Pillars of Wisdom\\"--which Bolt studied for his script--it's clear that he was a tortured soul.<br /><br />Such issues are, anyway, superfluous because this is not an historical documentary. It is an epic adventure story staged in a landscape of austere and perilous beauty. 0307346617|4.0|I wavered between a 4- and 5-star rating for this book; it's that good. This is my second time to read World War Z, and it was just as enjoyable the second time around.<br /><br />This story poses as a future history, chronicling the lives of those who have survived \\"the crisis,\\" a worldwide zombie plague. Several of you are probably already mousing toward the next review, assuming this is yet another zombie story, but let me promise that you're wrong. You'll have to suspend disbelief to the same extent you do in the works of Tolkien or Lucas, but Brooks does a nearly-equal job of captivatingly portraying the human experience in a unique situation.<br /><br />Brooks tells his tale by following a journalist across the world, as he catalogs true stories from survivors. Through that lens, Brooks is able to delve into fascinating ideas of how various nations would handle an apocalyptic crisis. Israel shuts down its borders and employs guard dogs to sniff out the infected, whereas China begins by assuming it can throw bodies at the incoming horde to slow their progress, until leaders realize that those bodies are reanimating and becoming yet another foot soldier for the opposition.<br /><br />One of the book's strengths lies in Brooks' ability to write in a variety of voices. The tale is really a collection of short stories, as the journalist interviews survivors, and remarkably, each survivor speaks uniquely. Brooks was able (generally) to write outside himself, providing a new voice and new emotions to each character. You'll read of heartbreak and loss in one chapter, and the next page will throw you into an interview with a mercenary whose bloodlust has long since rendered him unfit for normal suburban society.<br /><br />Ultimately, I give this book four stars rather than five for Brooks' few failure to step outside his own American ideology. In one chapter, a Cuban interviewee describes how the arrival of American refugees on Cuban soil slowly led the island nation to a transformation, the result of which was a national embracing of capitalism and democracy. Later, Brooks writes of an immigrant from El Salvador who had been deported before the crisis, but upon learning of the zombie outbreak fought his way up through Mexico and back to America -- not because he had any friends or family there, but simply because he loved the country so much. I don't mean to suggest that Brooks is a flag-waving American, but those two passages are illustrative of a few other places where the author lets national stereotypes cloud his writing.<br /><br />Outside of that solitary criticism, I highly recommend World War Z for what it is: a significant addition to a growing collection of zombie stories, a great work of fiction, and an often thought-provoking look at how the world would handle an international crisis of unprecedented proportions.to a transformation, the result of which was a national embracing of capitalism and democracy. I don't mean to suggest that Brooks is a flag-waving American, but those two passages are illustrative of a few other places where the author lets national stereotypes cloud his writing.<br /><br />Outside of that solitary criticism, I highly recommend World War Z for what it is: a significant addition to a growing collection of zombie stories, a great work of fiction, and an often thought-provoking look at how the world would handle an international crisis of unprecedented proportions.

Though it fails to surpass its predecessor, as an ambitious film with grand designs and elaborate themes, it mostly succeeds.<br /><br />Several years after the events of The Dark Knight, the legacy of Harvey Dent lives on as crime has been stifled in Gotham City and the wealthy and elite hold sway. Gotham is pristine and ready for a new age, and relics like Batman and even Commissioner Gordon are not needed anymore and obsolete.  A mysterious masked villain named Bane (Thomas Hardy) begins a calculated reign of terror which paralyzes the city.  But what is his motive and what does this have to do with Batman? As Gordon (Gary Oldman) pleads for the return of the caped crusader and the city cries out for salvation, Batman must overcome a very powerful Bane to save the city.  He is aided by his servant Alfred (Michael Caine), technical wizard Lucius (Morgan Freeman), and a young cop, John Blake (Joseph Gordon-Levitt), and bedazzled by two mysterious women, Selina (Anne Hathaway) and Miranda (Marion Cottilard).  But first, Bruce Wayne must return from the abyss to confront his past and battle for Gotham City's future.<br /><br />The briskly paced 2 hour 45 minute film is always engaging with memorable moments.  The opening sequence is a marvelous midair hijack right out of a Bond film. (Come to think of it, Nolan's got the Bond thing down as in the snowy climax of Inception.)  A scene where a doctor gives Wayne failing marks in his physical is amusing.  The climactic action between the forces of good and evil recalls the finale to Metropolis and the face off of its protagonist and antagonist are right out of Enter the Dragon.<br /><br />Nolan likes intricate puzzles; witness Momento and Inception.  He takes previous storylines including the convoluted ending of The Dark Knight and serves some nice twists here.  The film is a cleverly built trap, and Batman's fall from grace is a rapid decent into the depths of hopelessness and despair. To be great again, he has to literally and figuratively climb his way back; in a sense, the fates of the masked avenger and Gotham City are linked.<br /><br />The film is able to explore some interesting relationships both past and present, and duality of key characters is a recurring theme. For Wayne, Commissioner Gordon and Alfred are fatherly figures through his orphaned childhood and adult life.  Selina and Miranda are women with different backgrounds and agendas.  Blake, the idealistic, honest cop shares a common bond with Wayne.  Even Batman, the tortured, self sacrificing hero has a counterpart in Bane, the ultimate, super terrorist, who is intelligent, cunning, and ruthless.<br />With so many characters, it is remarkable how Nolan and screenwriter/brother Jonathan Nolan are able to weave together storylines featuring each role prominently.  In particular, the paradoxical Selina is a `cat burglar' and a survivor who has to learn how to trust another person.  Her interaction with Wayne always intrigues as they play out a kind of dance, and we wonder her true motives.  She is a kind of outsider and observer of the events that will determine the fate of Gotham City.<br /><br />The film boldly tackles major themes adding layer upon layer.  It's about past sins and morality: the great deception over Harvey Dent for the greater good presents risks and has severe consequences here.  Amid complacency there is class warfare: as Bane incites the masses and bolsters his numbers, the city faces a dichotomy of the proletariat versus the bourgeoisie.  The film also tests the concept of trust and faith: sometimes the ones we trust betray us while others may surprise us with their loyalty.<br /><br />There are minor missteps e.g. immaculate streets when the city's infrastructure is at a standstill.  A brief moment at a cafe at film's end reveals a bit too much of a truth when subtle reactions could have sufficed.  The film's tone is dark, angst filled with rare moments of humor. That's not a knock on the film but don't go expecting Avengers type of laughs.  There's also an amusing cameo in the role of President and a macabre return of a key villain.<br /><br />Hans Zimmer's minimalist pulse keeps the mood humming along.  Visuals are technically outstanding with minimal use of computer generated effects, and this is where it deviates from the more colorful, super heroes of The Avengers and X Men.  Here we have a hero and backdrop that is set in a believable world which heightens realism and credibility.<br /><br />Brimming with ideas as it challenges us in ways most action films movies don't, The Dark Knight Rises is a strong conclusion to the trilogy.  A deeply allegorical film that demands repeated viewings to truly appreciate its intricacies, it dares to bend and reinvent the mythology of the caped crusader, and although it doesn't quite trump The Dark Knight, which set the bar impossibly high, its vision at times carries its audience to wonderful, lofty heights.

But through constant and wild communication through intense sex AND talk you see how two people gain the courage to really find one another. Their layers of anxiety, fear, and desire for understanding keep getting peeled away layer by layer with deeper knowledge of one another's psyche.<br /><br />The sex really becomes a metaphor for how two people both learn about their own needs and desires as well as their mates in ways that most couples rarely explore. But just like any learning experience it seems to take each one moving baby steps forwards or backwards before they move on and grow and change or stay the same. The dance of pain, joy, peace, love, excitement, climax and sheer exhaustion reflect the way they and people in general feel when exposing their emotional and physical selves.<br /><br />It seems that each sex act in the book was a rumination of how one sees oneself and their mate from every angle physically, mentally, emotionally and spiritually. The guts and grit by which they tackle one another's issues over and over is played out in every sequence of talk and sex or sex and talk, over and over and over again.<br /><br />The book keeps \\"grinding\\" its way through to what seems like a more peaceful place for both of them by the end. However, if the author is trying to demonstrate that life is not a bowl of cherries unless you really are present each minute with those you love, I expect if the author decides to continue their story there will be continuing questions of commitment and how to gain trust and faith in those you love as well as the world one confronts each day.<br /><br />So enjoy the steamy sex throughout the book but also take the time to think about how the lives of the main characters and events surrounding them are a reflection of what we all cope with each day. Christian's character is a testament to how someone who was damaged early on and developed a negative coping style was able to stretch his emotional capacity in a positive way with the guidance of a loving partner and significant others. Anastasia was guided by her own desire to grow and find sexual and emotional maturity through her scrappy and sometimes annoying persistence. It would have been a very dull series of books if only about sex and didn't include the constant struggle between the two of them which slowly blossomed into understanding and love.

1573228737|5.0|Affinity is my favorite of Waters' books, perhaps because it's one of her most subtle and atmospheric.<br /><br />The book juxtaposes three Victorian \\"worlds\\": the world of working spiritualists, the world of women's prisons, and the world of upper middle-class Victorian lives. The first two are dramatic indeed, full of colorful theatrics in the first case and punitive logistics in the second. As always, Waters' research is impeccable, and allows her to craft a solid and convincing story around what could in other hands be difficult material.<br /><br />All three worlds are successfully evoked in the two intertwining first-person narratives that tell the story. The result is rich in emotion and detail, yet also bleak. Whether rich or poor, these are women with limited choices, who make the best moves they can given the restrictions that face them; in the end, both protagonists have made a difficult bargain, for all that one is even bleaker than the other. I've had friends call the book \\"depressing\\" for this reason, but I didn't find it so; the story arc feels right for these characters yet very different from the kind of life narratives we can craft today.<br /><br />Though the love story of the novel is one between women, the book's basic premise--an affluent young London woman makes charitable visits to Millbank prison, where a young spiritualist of complex if not dubious character is imprisoned--precludes virtually all physical intimacy. Perhaps for that reason, the story reads as human rather than lesbian per se, centered around a longing for love and recognition with which virtually any reader can identify.<br /><br />Margaret Prior's relationship with her repressive mother, Selina Dawes' varied relationships with her jailers: Relationships among women are explored in a variety of ways in the book, not only in the growing connection between its two protagonists. Power is always the issue at hand. Who has the power to choose a woman's life story? What must she do to redress the power imbalances to which her gender consigns her? How do women liberate each other, and imprison themselves and others? These questions sound dry, but Waters' many gifts shape them into compelling fiction.o to redress the power imbalances to which her gender consigns her? How do women liberate each other, and imprison themselves and others? These questions sound dry, but Waters' many gifts shape them into compelling fiction.

Protagonist Pak Jun Do (John Doe) is raised in an orphanage by his orphan master father in North Korea.  His mother's gone missing (taken to Pyongyang) and it's clear from the onset Jun Do struggles with who he is and where he comes from.  This struggle only increases once he's pulled from the orphanage to be a tunnel soldier (tae kwon do in the dark!).  Jun Do has it rough and the author spares no punches.<br /><br />Johnson does a great job describing the difficulties of being adrift without identity and what life in a regime such as North Korea can be like.  Things are very unpleasant for Do and some of the reading can be difficult if you're squeamish.  Do rises in the ranks and becomes a kidnapper for the regime (one with a heart, but one who does what it takes), an English translator on a fishing vessel (the best writing in a story full of good writing), a prisoner, and a murderer.  At each turn, brutal realities are revealed and the identity struggle within Do continues to fester.  This is made perhaps too obvious--or maybe not enough so--with the shift to Commander Ga (the book's Martin Guerre moment).  This is a fantastic novel full of adventure, great writing, understanding of human nature, and a glimpse into a brutal dictatorship.  A worthy read for certain.

1402790546|5.0|I have just finished reading Bomber which is the first non-fiction work that I have read of Len Deighton. Like many other people, I have grown up after WWII and my impressions of war are mainly due to the Hollywood version. There is no glamour in war and this book shows its horror in unrelenting detail.<br /><br />The book encapsulates war by illustrating 24 hours of the air war taking place over Europe in 1943. The protagonists are the British for it was our Royal Air Force that was doing the bombing and the Germans who were doing the defending.<br /><br />It is ironic that only recently, records have been published which reveal how inaccurate and noneffective our mass saturation type bombing instigated by 'Bomber' Harris really was. Most bombs missed their target by distances of nearly a mile and the main storyline of the book relates how an 'innocent' rural town was eradicated by the chance event of one of the RAF's pathfinders being shot down.<br /><br />That single event caused the other bombers to mistakenly release their bomb-loads on the town rather than on their target several miles away. The moral dilemma is an acute one. Whose fault was it? Of course it must have been the bombers' fault, but if the pathfinder had not been shot down then it might have illuminated its intended target and the innocents would have been saved.<br /><br />What is certain is that all of the people who died in Deighton's masterpiece were either innocent and blameless or just doing their duty irrespective of which side they were on. Our society, raised on images of Bhagdad suffering Shock and Awe should read 'Bomber' before we plunge headlong into another war. It is a frightening yet sobering read.[[ASIN:1453713700 That Will Do Nicely]]

Of course, comparing anything to the awful Guitar heaven album is not saying much...<br /><br />The bad news: the current Santana band line up is the weakest to date, especially in the area of drums, bass and keyboards.  Considering the shoes that they have to fill (Michael Shrieve, Leon Chancler and Graham Lear for drums, David Brown and David Margen for bass, Gregg Rolie and Tom Coster on keys), it is not an easy job, but it has been done countless times before without missing a beat.  These current guys play like sidekicks afraid of stepping on Carlos' toes.  The bass mix is so muddy that you can hardly pick it out, just this rumbling in the background.  And then the bass lines on most songs is SO BORING (single root notes) that it feels like some amateur, even though Benny Retveld is certainly capable technically of doing better (I saw him live).  Dennis Chambers is incapable of putting out the polyrhythms that his predecessors were churning out (listen to Graham's\\" heads hands and feet\\" solo).  What Carlos should do is just fire Dennis and hire his wife Cindy Blackman instead.  I saw her live and BOY can she put out some fine drumming from a very stripped down set.  I don't even know who is on keyboards nowadays, but all he can do at best is faithfully reproduce his predecessors' solos, not much imagination otherwise.<br /><br />If you listen to classic Santana, you will hear this ongoing interplay between bass, drums and keys layered upon the percussion bedrock that is the Santana sound.  This is mostly missing in this album except for maybe Canela, the 12th track.  Percussion does not even show up until Macumba, 2/3 of the way into the album.<br /><br />I do not think it is an ego thing, since throughout his career, Carlos has always surrounded himself with excellent players, and he thrives on what the other musicians put out and then takes it a step further.  Listen to the album by Abraxas Pool, which is essentially the original Santana band minus Carlos, and you will not hear much difference between that and what Carlos puts out.  So I would put the onus on the current band members to step up to the plate or get out of the way.<br /><br />The Native American theme is pretty idiotic because Carlos cannot pull off something like that.  Carlos can do Latin, he can do Jazz, he can do Inspirational but he can't do World Music even though he keeps trying on stage and failing.  Listen to him play reggae in tribute to his idol Bob Marley and you know what I mean.  The opening indian chants would have offended me if I were a Native American because it is so dreadfully clich.<br /><br />So what this album could be is Santana's Adult Contemporary Album.  If you give it a chance and listen to the latter half of the album with a date, if you haven't scored by the end of the night then either she is tone deaf or you are butt ugly.  Hey the guy is getting old, Carlos is allowed to have a mellow album if he chooses to do so, and let's give him a break and encourage him to continue putting out his own music instead of these crappy hip hop collaborations.  What Carlos needs to do is start a new band with musicians that can also write and then he can take us to the real guitar heaven...

B0060ZJ7BC|5.0|For anyone interested in acting as a craft, Philip Seymour Hoffman as coach Art Howe steals the show, with Jonah Hill as Peter Brand doing an amazing job of creating Brad Pitt's statistical analyst and assistant. Brad Pitt as the Oakland A's general manager Billy Beane gives an excellent performance but, like any Hollywood superstar, he never really stops being himself. Hoffman by comparison plays the Oakland A's coach to the point of losing himself in his character; we don't see Hoffman playing Howe, we see Art Howe. (Or at least a film character called Art Howe. The real life Howe doesn't like how he is portrayed in the film or the book.) The film offers a good example of the difference between excellent performing (Pitt) and excellent acting (Hoffman) and this would be enough to really like this film.<br /><br />But for a film lover there's more. Most of the dialogue is natural with very few dramatic one-liners; for tension, the director relies more on understated exchanges filled with the empty words that make up the conversations all of us have in daily life. There isn't much incidental music to artificially create moods, again the director relies on natural effects. If French director Eric Rohmer had ever directed a commercial American film, this is what he would have done. But don't let this scare you-- Moneyball isn't an art house film, it's a commercial film made with a few techniques borrowed from minimalist avant-garde directors.<br /><br />Another pleasant surprise is that I liked the movie better than I liked the book, even though I loved the book and read it years before seeing the movie. Maybe it's because I had no expectations this would ever be made into a movie, or if it did that it would be made into a documentary. Instead, director Bennett Miller did what Philip Kaufman did with Tom Wolfe's The Right Stuff: he took a factual book and turned it into a dramatic film.<br /><br />One word of warning: sport fans might find themselves disappointed at seeing players and plays relegated to the background of a story focused on numbers, statistics, and economics. But that is the point of the movie; where traditionalists argue that baseball is a human game involving hunches, intuition, performance and tense dramatic field plays, the film argue that results come from more abstract considerations. Baseball is really about thinking.<br /><br />Vincent Poirier, Montrealeeing players and plays relegated to the background of a story focused on numbers, statistics, and economics. 1469984202|5.0|Wow!  What a great story.  I found Howey's Wool Omnibus after I read and reviewed [[ASIN:161218362X Containment]].  Amazon's \\"Customers Who Bought This Item Also Bought\\" is to blame! The price for the five Wool books (as an Omnibus series) was way too low for the quality of science fiction provided.  In the same spirit and classic theme of dystopian stories like Orwell's 1984, and moving forward, modern and recent stories like The Hunger Games, Wool is the story of a world gone wrong.  A world where people know little and continue their lives both in that ignorance and hope for the future.  A world that is both expansive and confining, providing both mundane routine and mysterious details as the story progresses.<br /><br />The writing style and descriptive language is very readable and focuses on character development backed with simultaneous individual stories which allow the reader to piece together what is going on.  The story unfolds in a way where you learn a critical point (the first 10 pages reveal this style perfectly), then the reader can wonder how that came to be, and work towards the origin of that point.  Things are revealed at just the right pace, leaving you wanting for more while turning the pages, and really leaving few stones unturned. This makes for that page turning-\\"gotta stay up late\\" quality that I love when immersed in a great story. The story does take about 100 pages to get to that point, and those first pages want and need to be read, but the initial pace quickly picks up leaving a sense of not being able to turn pages fast enough.  This pacing may leave the reader surprised and satisfied at the same time.<br /><br />At this point after getting to read all five Wool books in a week rather than months, I can only imagine having to wait for the release of the next book during the initial publication of each book in 2011 and into 2012--as each book leaves you wanting more and unwilling to wait.  Even here at the end of book five of Wool, more is wanted.  That leaves \\"First Shift-Legacy\\" [[ASIN:1475154771 First Shift - Legacy (Part 6 of the Silo Series)]] as book six of the Wool silo series, which is more of a prequel with a new set of characters (purchased and cued up!). The title plays on what a reader already has learned from the first five Wool stories.<br /><br />Of course, waiting is not what you get with the Omnibus edition; you get all five of the books in one... and at a great price that is NOT reflective of what you reading.  Each of these books is worth the $5+ price that would total $25+ for the entire series if purchased separately--and I bet in the initial publication and for the relative anonymity of Hugh Howey, pricing might have been a barrier, especially without a hard publication along with the electronic versions.  The price was definitely something that made me interested in giving Wool a try--and now I am thinking I did not spend enough for the satisfaction gained.  To make up for it, I am buying the rest of what this author has to offer on Amazon.<br /><br />Wool is not a science and technology driven story, although the ecosystem is a technological and self contained marvel.  Wool is a story of a dystopian future that provides a sense of dread and claustrophobia, while dangling the carrot of freedom at well paced intervals. Wool is the story of human beings with their weaknesses, strengths, and in a sense of classic story telling, of how one individual can infect others with ideas that are both dangerous and liberating.  In short, classic sci-fi.<br /><br />Hugh Howey has a wonderful story telling capacity and hopefully has lots more to write in a productive career, while exposing readers to provocative, mind expanding, and thoughtful ideas in an entertaining and lively style.  Highly recommended.  Buy everything!e is wanted. It has helped me to a deeper knowledge of how miscommunication, misinterpretation, and simple misunderstandings can build over time, and slowly destroy relationships if left unchecked. I applied some of the knowledge in this book when interacting with a female roommate, and the bottom line is, it works.
