B008BIN9EW|5.0|I didn't think such a game could exist on the Android platform. Even now, after spending HOURS with the game I still only barely scratched the surface. UniWar HD is like a great turn based strategy game of old, focusing on combat and base capture. While there is no diplomacy or other advanced options here, the game is still outstanding.<br /><br />The premise of this app is both simple and complex. You have to take over the map by eliminating your opponents (up to EIGHT players at the same time) and capturing their (or neutral) bases. You must carefully use the units at your disposal to outmaneuver and gang up on your enemies, destroying them mercilessly. By taking these bases you will be awarded with more money each turn, allowing you to build more units, and laugh manically as your army rolls over the competition. If you do your job, you will crush your enemies, see them driven before you, and hear the lamentation of their women.<br /><br />There are three races in the game: The Kraleans, The Sapiens, and The Titans. Each race has completely unique units (eight of them) with different strengths, weakness, and abilities. The races are extremely well balanced, although my personal favorite so far are the Sapiens (Humans). I won't tell you why, because I think it will spoil your discovery moments with the game, which I also found fun.<br /><br />The graphics and sound here are as good as they need to be for a TBS game. The hexes are adequate enough to represent the terrain you are on and the units look good. The sound effects and music are also very satisfying. My only wish is that units and tiles were a bit more animated. While units do fidget from time to time, and there are attack (and base) animations - the tiles themselves are very bland. Spicing these up will go a long way because players spend a lot of their time staring at them.<br /><br />UniWar HD really is the complete package. There are plenty of options, including a very detailed Help option that will inform you on some of the more advanced tactics of the game. I suggest you go through it (or the campaign first) before jumping online because they will go over such things as medical bases, gang up bonuses, repairing, special abilities, etc. I made the mistake of venturing online first and got utterly annihilated (within a minute).<br /><br />Speaking of online mode, this is where the game shines. Up to eight players can play simultaneously, and you can have multiple games going while you wait for them to make their move (I advise against this until you have a better grasp of the game).<br />You can play anonymously or make an account, I suggest you sign up with UniWar for the sake of record keeping. Feel free to give them bogus info if you are concerned about privacy.<br />Using this account you can check detailed statistics on your wins, losses, ladder position, etc. Very cool and handy!<br /><br />If online is not your cup of tea, you can play against the AI and/or the excellent campaign which actually has a somewhat interesting story.<br />There is also the wonderful vs. mode, where you can play against your friends on the same phone (think hotseat option from PC TBS games). It's a great feature that gives UniWar HD a lot more bang for the buck.<br /><br />I can't say enough good things about this game. I absolutely love that they didn't use spyware infested junkware like OpenFeint or Scoreloop. Everything (such as scorekeeping, progression, leader boards, etc) is handled in-game here, so there are no unnecessary permissions. Good job devs!<br /><br />If I knew such a game existed, I would gladly pay full price for it. While steep at $4.99, it is very, very worth it if you at all enjoy turn based strategy games. This is quite simply the best FAOTD that Amazon has had in a while.<br />Go ahead and download it while it's free, and buy it if it's not. This game is amazing and will provide you with countless hours of entertainment (there are thousands of maps and theonline community is HUGE).
0375507256|1.0|Mitchell bamboozled us all. I don't care that the author is talented or has a great track record, or that two of the stories seem complex in this so-called novel, or that all of the stories seem totally unrelated but promise to be part of a masterful brilliant earth-shattering revelation. Because they're simply unrelated stories linked together with a rather cheap gimmick. Actually--unless you're already sold on all the gushing reviews, you start to realize halfway through that it's probably not going to be as \\"masterfully woven\\" together as you thought it would be. Once the narrative goes in reverse and you realize that wait--the only thing that links some of the totally unrelated characters is a birthmark (woo woo)--and wait--the supposed meta quality of some of them feels not smart/deft/devised, but rather, totally inauthentic (oh, one hilariously comedic monologue is *really* a masterful film from ages past that has been dug out of a vault but says nothing about anything else in the book!) or (oh! the mind-numbingly dull, meandering, and utterly unnecessary diary was published and found on the shelf of a random house in another story!)...etc etc...I tried to suspend belief and just trust Mitchell, but after a certain point, you find that trust was unearned and has panned out to be undeserved.<br /><br />I'd like to know if Mitchell claims to have conceived of these stories as a whole. Because in the end, it seems clear that he wrote a number of them as short stories--or had these disparate ideas--and then thought, oho, I could link them together with a few artificial devices and everyone would think it so complex they would be sure that it's a work of genius! I'd almost rather that he went back and artificially tried to connect them with more intent and a sense of necessity. Like I said, I did initially enjoy half the stories (the sci-fi tale of a clone in a futuristic Korea--not original at all, but interesting/well-written; the said comedic monologue, which was random and completely unbelievable but was redeemed by an absolutely hilarious narration; and the string of letters from a narcissistic but interesting musician--even if the story itself was pointless and self-indulgent), and hung in there assuming it would all start to become the bigger picture promised in the form. I even believed that the 'voice-throwing' stock spy novel story would pan out into something and so I read in good faith. I thought the post-apocalyptic story at the fulcrum would be something important (in spite of its total failure at ventriloquism and that the characterization was flat-out embarrassing and even ironic in the light of Mitchell's final sermon)--so I read in good faith.<br /><br />All that to say, I felt conned by the time I got to the last segment as soon as it became clear that even the slightest elegant connection that would show actual complex thought and purpose was not going to happen. And as that final dreary narrative of randomness (the diary-format story) came to its final heavy-handed, hollow moralizing conclusion, all I could say was \\"Seriously?!\\" Looking back at its parts, the whole thing falls apart. A savvy writer has proved that the world is easily won over by the sheen of superficial talent even in the absence of substance. You can write papers about its supposed themes and its 'brilliant' form all you want, but the story breaks down. I know many vehemently disagree and argue that I'm missing his crafty craft, but I'm not gonna lie and say I think a book that's 50% well-written prose is deserving of praise. There's plenty of well-written prose out there. I want a good story, and I don't think that's too much to ask for.<br /><br />***UPDATE*** Re: Tom Tykwer adaptation<br />If you've already read this book and hated it for similar reasons, thought I'd mention you might actually enjoy the film. Because basically Tom Tykwer, who understands storytelling, has projected his own meaning and connections into the narrative that are totally *not* present in the book (particularly by using the same actors in each segment, thereby suggesting a more sci-fi or mystical connection between characters that is non-existent in the book). Which is why he's succeeded in improving the story by leaps (not leaps and bounds, but sure, I'd vote for leaps) because it's actually cohesive by way of that visual character mechanism as well as really good editing.<br /><br />Which goes to show that if Mitchell had gone back and tried to analyze his stories as a whole and weave in some superficial but logical connections, he could have come out with a far more cohesive novel. To underscore this, I've observed most people who watched the movie first and then read the book projecting the film's connections into the book--so they come to each story already feeling like they know and believe in the connections between the characters.at are totally *not* present in the book (particularly by using the same actors in each segment, thereby suggesting a more sci-fi or mystical connection between characters that is non-existent in the book). Which is why he's succeeded in improving the story by leaps (not leaps and bounds, but sure, I'd vote for leaps) because it's actually cohesive by way of that visual character mechanism as well as really good editing.<br /><br />Which goes to show that if Mitchell had gone back and tried to analyze his stories as a whole and weave in some superficial but logical connections, he could have come out with a far more cohesive novel. 0451167716|4.0|Unquestionably among the best fictional treatments of the Mafia, \\"The Godfather\\" is top-of-the-line escapist literature, as well as an invaluable companion piece to the movie (or vice versa).  There's the requisite and appropriate amount of action, but it's not excessively violent or ridiculously gory (though most of the sex scenes weren't needed and were pretty silly).  The majority of the characters have depth and are well drawn.  A notable exception is Kay Adams Corleone (Diane Keaton's colorless interpretation of Kay renders the book version of the character positively three-dimensional in comparison), and I would have much preferred it if Michael Corleone had been able to stay married to the luscious and lively Apollonia (and I'm sure he felt the same way).  The overall narrative is fine, though there are a few issues.  First, the novel is somewhat longer than it needs to be.  Book IX, for instance, while hardly lengthy, is completely unnecessary and anti-climactic.  The novel should have ended with the Hemingwayesque \\"She went back into the kitchen and wept.\\"  Also, there's an occasional problem in continuity, some stuttering, and the author sometimes loses control of his material.  But where Mario Puzo really went off track was in Book II, and indeed in any and all parts of the novel where Johnny Fontane receives more than passing notice.  After all, Fontane is at best a peripheral figure.  Why all the tedious biographical detail?  I was particularly annoyed because Fontane is clearly a fictional representation of Frank Sinatra, whom I've always detested.  He was a great singer in his prime, and he had some acting ability, but he was basically a punk and a hood wanna-be all his life.  A vulgar little creep, with a mean streak deep and wide, he tried to come across as \\"classy\\" (a quality absolutely alien to him) by wearing a tuxedo on a more or less perpetual basis.  Also, Sinatra's attitude toward and treatment of women was deplorable -- the man was a cad.  He knew, deep down, that few women would have looked at him twice (or even once) if not for his fame.  Off the top of my head, the only thing I can say in his favor is that the disgracefully forgotten boxer, Tami Mauriello, was his favorite prizefighter.  OK, enough of Fontane/Sinatra.  But I should add that a similar, although lesser, problem arises with the endlessly explored relationship of Jules Segal and Lucy Mancini -- nothing but filler.<br /><br />\\"The Godfather\\" was meant to entertain, and it succeeds in doing just that.  And it's fun connecting the dots, as it were.  By that, I mean figuring out what real-life people Puzo fictionalized -- e.g., Moe Greene is Bugsy Siegel.  Despite a few problems...recommended.

0307477479|5.0|I've always enjoyed novels-in-stories. For my money, [[ASIN:0375507256 David]] [[ASIN:0375724508 Mitchell's]] [[ASIN:349804513X books]] are the best examples of how novels-in-stories can be used to great effect. Others from [[ASIN:1400032822 Palahniuk]] to [[ASIN:0312425023 Cunningham]] have attempted them with some differing measures of success. Still though, the idea of showing how separate stories can intertwine and coalesce into a greater whole is a fascinating and ever-more-popular element of culture (even the once-lauded [[ASIN:B0042YZGB8 Lost]] is an example of this).<br /><br />Egan's own contribution to this idea may not have deserved the Pulitzer, but it's still one of the best books I've read in recent years. The stories follow a group of people from the sixties up into a distant future where words like \\"identity,\\" \\"real,\\" and \\"change\\" are no longer definable outside of their ironic meanings. The characters have experiences from the deserts of America to the wilds of Africa, and although they sometimes endure radically different situations, they all struggle with the same need to find themselves and to fight the inexorable plod of time.<br /><br />It can get confusing at times. The stories are not arranged in any kind of chronological order, and since they involve many different lives, the cast list can be cumbersome to some readers. The only solid element of the book that ties them all together is a connection with music, but this is tangential to the characters themselves. Even when the events take place in a tech-heavy future, Egan avoids prolonged exposition about time and place, letting the effects and impacts of the places resonate within the characters themselves. They are all rich, evocative, and fully realized by Egan's pitch-perfect and finely tuned prose.<br /><br />If you wanted only to read this book as a collection of stories, it would still be just as fruitful. If you want to do the homework of tracing lines of connection, the domino effect of actions rippling forward through lives, it adds an extra vein of meaning and satisfaction to the book's scope. Enjoyable on multiple levels, Egan's novel is the kind of immediately accessible experiment that is easy to warm up to, and which leaves behind that telltale ache of regret (sort of like with life itself) when it seems to finish before its time.of actions rippling forward through lives, it adds an extra vein of meaning and satisfaction to the book's scope. B008JMKN4Y|3.0|Hmmm!  I've set out to put down my thoughts about this book a few different times and keep hesitating which is not like me when I finish a book.  I asked to receive this book because I read so many wonderful reviews and because of the blurb on it.  I've been hearing about the fairly new genre of New Adult and thought this would be a nice read to introduce me to it.<br /><br />The story takes place on a small college (and for those who are wondering who've read the story already- yes, there are small colleges that can make one feel like they are living in a fish bowl where everyone knows your business because I attended one) when a bad boy falls for an apparently nice innocent girl.  Her rebuffs intrigue and delight him rather than put him off so he lays out a bet that she loses in which she must (when I say 'must' I gotta say that I mean she is choosing to stay though she tries to convince everyone otherwise) stay with him for a month in which they both form a friendship that defies the understanding of all.  Both parties, Travis and Abby, want more, but there are many complications that will and do pull them apart when they do give it a chance for more.  Their relationship is what gives the book its title.<br /><br />Now for my opinion- which is exactly what this is, MY opinion.  I state that because there are so many others with vastly different opinions (like always- hehe).  I had a love-hate thing going with this story.  In fact, it took me much longer than usual to finish reading it because of how it pulled me back and forth between 'can't put it down to see what happens' next to the very dangerous (and expensive because I read it on my kindle) temptation to throw it against a wall in frustration.<br /><br />What aroused such feelings?  This is the part that will contain spoilers just so I can explain my impressions of the story.  So read on at your own risk!!!!<br /><br />First of all, I love a good opposites attract/ wrong, but right for each other story.  I also loved the camaraderie with all the friends in the story.  I adored Travis' family. And some of the plot conflict was riveting.  But- and its a big 'but' because the story is entirely from this character's point of view and she's a main character- I hated Abby a great deal of the time.  Small third world dictators could take lessons for torture of the emotional kind from her.  And she was so good at it that she was able to take down several people (some multiple times) in this story.  While she was busy- first being in denial that Travis loved her and she, him(in the face of everyone telling her the same thing)- and then being in full self-protection mode with the hot/cold treatment to Travis (don't want to get hurt/I'm scared- want guy- but don't want to get hurt again/I'm scared of my own feelings loop) and then finally in her spiteful pride phase (you screwed up though at the time you owed me nothing and I'm going to hold it against you no matter how broken and miserable I can see you are), she ripped out a guy's heart more than once, broke up her best friend's romance because said BFF loyally sided with her against her own boyfriend who was the cousin to Travis (broken hearted guy mentioned earlier), and toyed (not on purpose-much of the time- to be fair) with perfectly nice guys giving the impression that she was available then pulled back from them.  I really struggled to buy her hang-ups particularly with all the 'I'm the one who's bad for him and I'll ruin him' even after I was given the explanation about her past dealings that led to these thoughts. I don't normally include book comparisons, but just to help explain the Abby character here.  She reminded me strongly of the Bella Swan character in New Moon and Eclipse with her dealings with Jacob (I need you, but we can never be more than friends behavior).<br /><br />Now, before I appear entirely unfair and fixated about Abby, let me just say that I don't think its wrong for Abby to take a 'lets be friends' approach when she first gets started out with Travis or a little later making it a 'lets take our time'  when they enter a relationship because he is into some serious stuff- bare knuckle fighting for cash, sleeping around with a reputation (that is earned) for one night stands and having emotional issues of neediness, anger and jealousy.  But, you see, her 'lets be friends' was her lying to herself and everyone else as evidenced by her thoughts and actions.  And his emotional issues many times are a direct result of her behavior.  Travis was truly a bad boy (personally, I'm not enamored with this in real life- showing my age here) and I would not have faulted her caution if it was an honest caution that was not laced with hypocrisy.<br /><br />As to the other aspects of the story, (I am about to confuse you when I say that I loved it) I loved when things were going good and Travis and Abby were together and he was trying so hard to change.  The deft touch of friends and lovers really struck a nice chord with me.  I really enjoyed the dynamics of the double dating couples between the two cousin sets of Travis and Abby and Shep and America.  Shep, America and Finch were great secondary characters.  The other scenes that were touching was the two spent with Travis' dad and brothers.  I'm a huge lover of meeting the family in the story.  Finally, getting into what hurt was in Abby's past just made me feel for her.  The excitement of Travis' fights, the trip to Vegas the first time and the fire were page turning moments.<br /><br />I can certainly see why this is touted as a wonderful read because it does draw an emotional response.  When a book engages me with any emotion then I think the author did a pretty good job even if the book's not for me.<br /><br />So, my foray into Beautiful Disaster left me a little emotionally drained, but I can and do recommend this book to those who enjoy a good angsty read that leaves them wrung out, but satisfied in the end.  I will caution this as a recommend for an impressionable teen because there is much about the relationship that is not commendable on both Travis and Abby's parts.<br /><br />Thank you to Net Galley for providing the ARC copy for review purposes.nds' approach when she first gets started out with Travis or a little later making it a 'lets take our time'  when they enter a relationship because he is into some serious stuff- bare knuckle fighting for cash, sleeping around with a reputation (that is earned) for one night stands and having emotional issues of neediness, anger and jealousy. B006LQQ1GE|2.0|Like pretty much everyone else around the world who's either bought or borrowed this album, I fell a little bit in love with \\"Somebody That I Used to Know\\" when I heard it on the radio.  In a music world oversaturated with auto tune, electronic dance music, and just generally poor musicianship, Gotye's fresh take on pop music was a breath of fresh air.  \\"Somebody That I Used to Know\\" is undeniably unique, and if the rest of this album is even half as good as that one particular song, it MUST be hit, right?<br /><br />Wrong.  Billy Corgan recently made a statement about the music industry:  \\"If you're just going to create an album of singles, then I guess you don't care.  ...The public's interest in albums has diminished, it sort of rewards those people who can come up with the one song.  You have, in essence, the wrong end of the telescope.  It's like guilt by implication.  So if you come up with this one song, people think, Oh, this artist is so great!\\"  He must have been listening to this record before he made that statement, because it describes this album PERFECTLY.  As a single, \\"Somebody That I Used to Know\\" is gold.  It's unique, it's quirky, it utilizes instruments not heard by 99.9% of other popular artists, and it seems to tell a story.  However, it doesn't really fit into the framework of the album, 'Making Mirrors'.  More to the point, none of the songs on this album fit into anything even remotely resembling a framework.  'Making Mirrors' is little more than a collection of 12 singles packaged as an album.<br /><br />Now, if you're into that, fine, but I have a major problem with 'Making Mirrors' becoming a hit album based on the popularity of one song.  Gotye dabbles in a lot of different sounds and musical genres in these 12 songs, but they don't really fit in with or play off of each other.  They just...are.  I also have a major problem with Gotye touring major arenas all across the country based on the success of \\"Somebody That I Used to Know,\\" but that's another argument altogether.<br /><br />I guess you could say I agree with Billy Corgan, and I feel like this album is just...lazy.  What ever happened to bands/artists recording and amassing a huge collection of songs then piecing those songs together into a cohesive record?  I'm not saying it has to be a long narrative like Pink Floyd's 'The Wall' or a concept album like The Sword's 'Warp Riders', but to haphazardly slap together a bunch of songs and call it an album spits in the face of music lovers everywhere.that's another argument altogether.<br /><br />I guess you could say I agree with Billy Corgan, and I feel like this album is just...lazy. 0345803485|1.0|Here's what you can learn from \\"Fifty Shades of Grey\\" if you are an aspiring author who wants to write a GOOD book:<br /><br />If writing a fan fiction, leave it in the world of fan fiction. If you absolutely CANNOT do this, and MUST publish it as an original novel, please do more than simply change characters' names and locations. The only thing here that separates Christian from Edward Cullen is the lack of fangs. The only thing that separates Ana from Bella Swan is...wait...nothing.<br /><br />Go through your entire novel and take note of how many times you use the same words over and over again, and then eliminate at least 90% of the instances. While we are on this subject, do NOT use the word \\"murmur\\" multiple times per page. In fact, don't use it at all, especially when it makes no sense. Examples: \\"Hello, Ana, nice to meet you,\\" Christian's mother murmurs. \\"Nice to meet you too, Christian's mother,\\" I murmur. \\"Oh my God, the building is on fire!\\" I murmur. \\"I just saved a bunch of money on my car insurance by switching to Geico,\\" Christian murmurs. (Some of these sentences may have been fabricated for dramatic effect, and to get my point across.)<br /><br />I don't care how sweet and innocent your Mary-Sue main character is, if she is over age 12, she should never realistically refer to her nether region as \\"down there\\" - especially multiple times! It will make people question whether it's the character or the AUTHOR who is having a hard time using more adult terminology. Either way, it's unacceptable and makes it really hard to take the sex scenes seriously.<br /><br />Do not make your male and female main characters perfect, okay? Christian Grey is young, impossibly beautiful, flies helicopters, plays the piano better than Bach, is a successful businessman worth 16 bajillion dollars, can bring a girl to climax by a single touch or look, smells like roses, and has an appendage that would make Ron Jeremy feel inadequate. In other words, this guy is Fifty Shades of Unrealistic. And Ana, although self-described as being plain and boring and klutzy (hmm, sound like anyone else you may know? *coughBellaSwan*cough*) is adored by any man she meets, and her milkshake apparently brings all the boys to the yard. The fact that somebody like Christian Grey would be so enthralled with a stuffy, plain-looking prude like Ana is beyond far-fetched. At least Edward had the excuse of Bella's blood smelling like a Big Mac.<br /><br />Please don't ever refer to your perfect specimen of a male main character as an \\"Adonis\\". This should be left to fan fictions ONLY! Also, make your female character shut up about her \\"inner goddess\\", unless your novel is about a woman who becomes possessed by the spirit of an actual goddess.<br /><br />Find an editor, a professional one, to read your work before it is published. Have them make changes and corrections. Just because your novel is \\"erotic fiction\\", that doesn't give it a free pass to be horribly written.<br /><br />I could keep going on and on about all of the amazing Writing DONT'S you can learn from in this book, but this review is already too long. Is the book good? No. Is it entertaining? Yes. But for me, it was not entertaining in the same way it was entertaining for thousands of other women out there. If nothing else, this book has really helped me with the editing process of my own novels. I figure, I'll just do the opposite of what E.L. James does, and I should be all set.es of Unrealistic. 0307592731|5.0|What a treat to finally read an outdoor, wilderness, adventure novel written by a woman!  Cheryl Strayed captures what most male outdoor writers miss - the fact that being in wilderness is about an interpersonal journey, not conquering nature or taking something from it to become your own.  Strayed allows readers into her healing journey that occurs on the Pacific Crest Trail.  The only criticism or thing that irked me a bit is why do books get only written by people who are CLUELESS in the wilderness??  (Into the Wild, Timothy Treadwell, etc...).  Strayed's lack of research before her epic hike, and the dumb, dumb, dumb life threatening things she does (like drink all her water before reaching a now dry water station) is kind of annoying, and I honestly don't know why more books are not written about competent outdoors people!  Other than that, the book is wonderful and Strayed does wisen up along the journey, well, sort of, she continues to make mistake after mistake, but at least writes about knowing she is an idiot!  Strayed's voice is strong, unique, raw and compelling. You don't want to put the book down. I could have done without quite all the childhood belly aching, but that really is what her journey is about - overcoming a bad childhood with an abusive father (no wonder its an Oprah book club pick since Oprah is addicted to child abuse books which I am frankly sick to death of...).  However Wild didn't annoy me like some whine-about-my-bad-upbringing tales...And it is beyond refreshing for someone like me, avid hiker, camper, adventure, to read about a WOMAN doing these things!  Her perspective as a woman rubs off on the entire book.  This is a great, fun read that any adventurous soul will enjoy.  You will cheer on Strayed from beginning to end.  Let's get more books like this published!!

B008TRUDAS|5.0|Read this and other reviews at [...]<br /><br />This was one of those series where I finished it in record time, felt like someone punched me in the gut and then proceeded to immediately Google and then stalk the author to find out if there was going to be more, because I NEEDED MORE! It was so much more interesting to learn about the characters through their poetry; definitely a unique view, aside from typical dialogue or internal monologue. 159102594X|4.0|It used to be, and not all that long ago, that \\"fantasy\\" meant Tolkien. And maybe C. S. Lewis. And then, around the late 1970s, a whole slew of new varieties of fantasy rather suddenly began to appear -- darker, sometimes in an urban setting, featuring protagonists who weren't especially high and noble, nor clothed in glittering raiment and carrying shining swords. Sometimes there weren't even any identifiable Good Guys, just as the heroes of noir detective stories weren't especially Good. (Just successful.) I find most noir fantasy to be a lot more interesting, frankly, than Tolkien and his many clones. It all seems a lot closer to the so-called real world. Abercrombie is new at this but he seems to have a knack for sword-and-sorcery with grit added in.<br /><br />There are three principal characters: Logen, known as \\"Ninefingers\\" for readily apparent reasons, is a Northerner, which translates as \\"barbarian\\" to those in the more traditionally civilized Southern cultures. He's managed to survive into middle age (his mantra in a tight situation is \\"Not dead yet\\") but he's also getting tired. Now homeless and alone, following his most recent escape from those who want him dead, he's following a summons by Bayaz, First of the Magi, who looks more like a village butcher than a wizard -- but he's really not someone you want to annoy. Second is San dan Glotka, once a champion swordsman and a colonel in the army of the Union, before he was captured by the Imperials and tortured for more than a year. Now, crippled and in constant agony, he has become an inquisitor, a profession at which he appears to be very capable. (Takes one to know one when it comes to torture, apparently.) His own constant thought is \\"Why do I do this?\\" He sometimes makes the higher-ups in the Inquisition nervous because he's entirely past being frightened by pain or even threat of death. Finally, there's Capt. Jezal dan Luthar, a typically narrow-minded and empty-headed young member of the aristocracy who has been training for months for the annual Summer  Contest, in which the year's greatest swordsman will be determined by competitive elimination. Such men in the past have frequently risen to high office in the Union, so it's largely a matter of ambition. Luthar, however, is also capable of change, given the right motivation -- which, in this case, is the rather common and coarse sister of his colleague, Major West. Even though their social stations are vastly different and marriage is not an option, he can't help falling in love with her.<br /><br />The background to the story is the growth in power among the Northerners, now being led by their first-ever king, who craves victory over their old enemy, the Union. At the same time, there's a new and viciously successful emperor in power on the Union's opposite border, their traditional enemy. The king's younger officers, plus most of the nobility, assume both their enemies can be beaten in time for lunch. Bayaz knows different, and so do the more thoughtful decision-makers. And that's where this first volume of the trilogy ends, with everyone going off to war -- or almost everyone. Bayaz has a quest in mind that could Change Everything, and he recruits Ninefingers and Luthar to assist him. Glotka, meanwhile is being sent off to shore up the loyalty of a shaky province bordering the empire. But one gets the definite impression that all these major figures will be coming together before long.<br /><br />Abercrombie treats his characters as \\"real world\\" people, even those who are weird even to their peers, and he has a talent for internal dialogue. The narrative is a little stiff in places, but he has done an excellent job for a first-timer. And I have the second and third volumes already waiting on my bookshelf.who has been training for months for the annual Summer  Contest, in which the year's greatest swordsman will be determined by competitive elimination. B0083GQ2A0|3.0|I rented \\"The Grey\\" and was really looking forward to it.  I had heard a lot about it, and only one or two of the people I spoke to said they didn't really like it. My intrigue began to wane however, and eventually deflated to disappointment in a movie that had a lot of potential.  Here's the good and the not so good.<br /><br />Good:<br />Interesting story. While one doesn't expect today's action movies to be extremely realistic, the wolves portrayed in this movie were the size of bears.  Again, I know it's a movie and maybe the size of the wolves could be said to be symbolic, but much of the tension and suspense of a movie like this is generated through the audience identifying something in the movie that could be real, something that could actually happen.  Being chased down by wolves the size of grizzlies is not very likely to happen unless you've decided to partake of certain street pharmaceuticals.  My other large complaint and dissatisfaction with the film was that it felt like it couldn't decide what kind of movie it was going to be.  At times the movie felt more like a mindless action movie.  Awesome!  I love action movies.  But then only a scene later the movie becomes poetic and deeplyepic.  Which is fine too, but these two elements were never melded together so the movie becomes choppy, going from slaughtering things with no remorse to reciting rhymes and poems the main character's father taught him.  The most frustrating part about the movie was that it had so much potential to be so much more.  If the director had had a more clear vision of what he wanted the movie to be, and stayed with it, it could have been a great movie.  Instead we're left with an attempt at capturing a great classic of conflict: man vs. nature, but with distractions and no clear path the whole way.  The circumstances leading up to the ending of the movie seemed to be haphazardly thrown together, but the actual ending of the movie was well done.  It was a huge reason why I was frustrated with the unrealized potential of the film.  If the director had used the ending, which was just open ended enough to leave you thinking after the movie has been returned, and built everything around that, it would have been great.  Alas I think the days of story being more important than explosions and giant wolves are gone.  Gone are the Hitchcocks.  I was just hoping for more.

It is all way over-the-top neon colors, headbands, leg warmers and a heavy rotation of one hit wonder hits such as Come On Eileen and I'll Melt With You. No one seems to acknowledge that most of us never dressed that way and that though those songs were popular; there were volumes and album stacks of other music that was played and enjoyed. There is a reason that the term alternative became a music genre, just as there is a reason John Hughes movies still possess relevancy - there were memorable and meaningful things in the eighties - scads of it.<br /><br />One of the elements that Ready Player One gets so right - I mean, completely right - is the eighties. From the film choices, to the music, to video and in-person games, to books and television, it is spot on.  The book itself is filled to the brim with pop culture references. Not since reading Rob Sheffield's books have I felt so personally drawn back to my adolescence, and so connected to the time and place of what it was really like (at least for me).<br /><br />The other thing that I truly believe Cline gets so right is the draw the internet holds to society and sociology, the enormous impact social media, online role playing games, and the shrinking of the world with a click of the keyboard has had on our life now - and in the case of Ready Player One, what it could do to the not so distant future.<br /><br />Wade is a young, desolate orphan who lives in both poverty and family neglect. The world he resides in is post-apocalyptic (or perhaps post-global warning/post-financial crash/financial ruin), and the majority of society are leaving in poverty. A socially inhibited, possibly Asperger/autistic, technological genius, James Halliday, with the help and partnership of his best (only) friend have created a literal oasis called the Oasis, which is a computer generated universe - scratch that, universes - that work like a next generation World of Warcraft.<br /><br /> The Oasis is available to everyone, costing only pocket change to join up, and once a user name and avatar are created one can live their entire existence online - including school, career, relationships, competitions and even sex. A user is fit with a haptic suit and gloves which physically make one feel like they are not playing in a game setting, but living within it.<br /><br />The appeal to this is hard to deny.<br /><br />When the creator of the Oasis falls ill he decides to create an intricate, near impossible mysterious puzzle of a game, a coveted egg to be found at the very end. The winner of said game will inherit all of his fortune, and control of the Oasis. Here is where the story begins.<br /><br />Oasis, being available to nearly anybody, means that for a while nearly everyone was trying to solve the game and find Halliday's egg. Time went by, though, and this was not an amateur treasure hunt, so many but the truly persistent (i.e. obsessed) continued this quest - the persistent and the big corporation. As big business is wont to do, the biggest corporation in the world wants its hands on the egg, and in turn Oasis, so that they can turn it into a profitable, only for those who can afford it space. And the persistent, or gunters, as they are called - well, they want it to prove they can solve it, and in the case of Wade, in order to escape his nowhere life.<br /><br />Wade, or Parzival as his avatar is called, has a best friend who he has never met in \\"real life\\", Aech (pronounced as the letter H). He also has one hell of a huge crush on a smart, sarcastic, pop culture maven of a girl hiding behind her avatar, deemed Art3mis. Though Wade was easy to care about, and root for, I have to say it was Art3mis and Aech that I really loved.<br />The adventure is fast-paced, addicting and impossible to stop reading. Attimes I felt my heart racing along with the three gunters, wondering how they would survive. Other times I cringed and grew irritated with their choices, often catching myself talking to the pages of the book, as if the characters would hear me and take heed.  And, in the end (which I will not spoil), I was touched by a reminder that I try to hold true, about life and love, choices and risk, and persistence.<br />This book is categorized as young adult, a genre, or category, an admitted favorite of mine. That said, I hope the category does not scare off older readers, as I do believe that the reader who grew up in the eighties may enjoy it just that much more than a younger aged reader. Especially moments, like one of my favorites, when a quest in the game is having to act out an entire film, word and action for word and action - the film being 1983's WarGames.<br /><br />Rumor has it Ready Player One is soon to hit the movie screen, which I am both excited and apprehensive about. I hope they can capture the pacing, the imagery and the overall feeling of being digitally and emotionally immersed in a created reality without making it a movie full of badly animated avatars. I hope the humanity is kept intact, as it is in this awesome book.times I felt my heart racing along with the three gunters, wondering how they would survive. Other times I cringed and grew irritated with their choices, often catching myself talking to the pages of the book, as if the characters would hear me and take heed.  And, in the end (which I will not spoil), I was touched by a reminder that I try to hold true, about life and love, choices and risk, and persistence.<br />This book is categorized as young adult, a genre, or category, an admitted favorite of mine. That said, I hope the category does not scare off older readers, as I do believe that the reader who grew up in the eighties may enjoy it just that much more than a younger aged reader. Especially moments, like one of my favorites, when a quest in the game is having to act out an entire film, word and action for word and action - the film being 1983's WarGames.<br /><br />Rumor has it Ready Player One is soon to hit the movie screen, which I am both excited and apprehensive about. I hope they can capture the pacing, the imagery and the overall feeling of being digitally and emotionally immersed in a created reality without making it a movie full of badly animated avatars. I hope the humanity is kept intact, as it is in this awesome book.

030758836X|5.0|\\"Gone Girl,\\" by Gillian Flynn (Crown 2012), is simply the best book I've read this year. Flynn is a relatively new author, having published only three novels so far. Her first, \\"Sharp Objects\\" came out in 2007, and \\"Dark Places\\" came out three years later. \\"Gone Girl\\" was published in June of this year, and has risen meteorically as an Amazon bestseller. What makes this novel so worth reading is what makes all great novels worth reading: an engaging story told with superb writing.<br /><br />\\"Gone Girl\\" is a story about marriage in the extreme, and like so many far-out tales of fictional relationships, it serves to illuminate the constituents of real marriage in real life: what is expected, what is owed, and what will be taken if not given. \\"Gone Girl\\" is Hemingway writing \\"War of the Roses.\\" It's a funny, intriguing, and revealing story about those aspects of a dysfunctional marriage that remind us the worst damages often come from the hands of those we trust the most. Gillian Flynn holds nothing back.<br /><br />There are two main characters, Nick and Amy. Amy, the famous inspiration behind the Amazing Amy series of children's books co-authored by her parents, goes missing and is presumed dead. An investigation is launched by the police and the FBI (and the reader, of course) to find the kidnapper/killer. Naturally, the husband is always suspect number one. What ensues is a game of cat and mouse that will have you saying OMG! with each new chapter and each new twist. I wish I could go on, but I can't describe the story any further without spoiling it for you. So, I will leave it to you to read the book for the rest of it. Nevertheless, the second half of this book's greatness is the writing.<br /><br />When it comes to the writing, Flynn is a master at using the English language to convey her clever insights. This is the hallmark of an expert writer, and Flynn is the definitive expert. Her sense of pacing, tension, suspense, transitions, and diction are on par with some of the greatest writers I've ever read. I honestly believe we'll see a lifelong career from this author as she goes on to write books that become top movies and one day earn her a Pulitzer Prize. In fact when I chose to review \\"Gone Girl,\\" I chose it over the 2011 Pulitzer Prize winning author, Jennifer Eagan, simply because the writing of Gillian Flynn was more captivating than what I found in \\"A Visit From the Goon Squad.\\"<br /><br />I highly encourage anyone looking for a great mystery thriller to grab a copy of \\"Gone Girl.\\" I can easily stake my reputation on the assertion that you'll not be disappointed with the work of this American author. Now is the time to start following her. Gillian Flynn is one of the best.<br /><br />Way to go, girl!<br /><br />--Edward Gordon<br />The Novel Reporth some of the greatest writers I've ever read. 1451648537|5.0|Walter Isaacson had a tough task here - compiling the story of a near-mythic individual, one whose life was thought to be known (every move and utterance parsed and scrutinized) and yet still remained unknown (his family life remained deeply private), and releasing it in the days when that mythologizing was stoked to a fever pitch.  Despite that pressure, Isaacson has produced an outstanding piece of work.  It's as readable as a novel.  In many ways, its subject's life does have the setbacks and triumphs of a piece of fiction.  The protagonist is as complex as any character that the best writer could conjure up.<br /><br />I'm struck by the balance of the book.  It clocks in at 570 pages and could easily go much, much longer.  Entire libraries have been written about some of the subjects covered here: the birth of Apple; Sculley and Jobs; Gates and Jobs; Jobs at NeXT; the return of the King; the birth of the iPod and so on.  Thinking about one small story from the book: the tale of Apple shifting from Motorola chipsets to Intel.  I thought, wow, I could read a book about that subject alone.  And there's surely a book's worth of material there.  Yet, Isaacson dives in just deep enough on each subject to give you the essence of the man.  He has to do that.  Otherwise, he winds up with 1,500 pages.  I'd read that as would some others, but most of the public will barely tolerate a 570-page work.<br /><br />Second and more importantly in terms of balance, even though Jobs solicited Isaacson directly, he encouraged the author not to hold back in terms of presenting the bad along with the good.  [Jobs' wife tells Isaacson in the book's prologue that her husband had a \\"messy\\" life in a lot of areas.]  In a world filled with anti-Jobs screeds and pro-Jobs encomiums, Isaacson nails the genius/a--hole dichotomy at the center of this guy's life.  Besides tales of brilliance, focus and relentlessness, Jobs' inherent meanness is a constant theme of the book.  As the epilogue states, \\"[e]ven his family members wondered whether he simply lacked the filter that restrains people from venting their wounding thoughts or willfully bypassed it.\\"  For a guy that lumped all people into \\"hero or sh-thead\\" categories, Isaacson's work at detailing Jobs own dichotomous nature without appearing to take a side is a feat of dexterity and integrity.es, \\"[e]ven his family members wondered whether he simply lacked the filter that restrains people from venting their wounding thoughts or willfully bypassed it.\\"  For a guy that lumped all people into \\"hero or sh-thead\\" categories, Isaacson's work at detailing Jobs own dichotomous nature without appearing to take a side is a feat of dexterity and integrity.

B008EL4YX6|5.0|Note: I got this as the free app of the day on my Kindle Fire.  I would happily pay a buck for it.<br /><br />Ichi is a variation on the 'move through all the boxes' kind of game. It has a simple yet elegant look to it. The game play is straightforward, but still challenging.<br /><br />Think of it as a kind of directed pinball, where you want to hit each of a set of targets. In each level, the ball is in an odd shaped room, moving in straight lines, rebounding off (some) walls and obstacles. Your goal is to pass the ball through every target on the level without letting the ball leave. To do this, you tap the screen to rotate all the controllable bumpers on the screen by 90 degrees at a time, which in turn changes the direction the ball bounces off of them. Your goal is to hit all the targets with the minimum number of taps.<br /><br />The game has a nice, clean look to it, almost like a blueprint. It works great on my fire, and the controls are responsive. The level design ramps up  nicely, from trivial to diabolically difficult. For each level you get a letter grade (based on the number of taps you used) which tells you when you've done great, or when there's a better way to solve the level. B002LLE3Q8|5.0|If ever there was a pre-digital recorded work that needed the dynamical range afforded by digital recording, this is it.<br />Steven Wilson so clearly grocks Crimson.<br />One of my favorite albums, first heard in 1971.<br />This is a very minor rewrite of my original review, with thanks to a fellow Crimson fan's expert critique.<br /><br />I direct my thoughts at those who don't know it.<br /><br />Ignore this harbinger of \\"Prog\\" at your musical peril. Recall that \\"Prog\\" did not exist in 1969. And we soon after talked of \\"Precision Rock\\"<br />I would posit that this album, for all of its alleged faults, really did pop up out of nothing in some senses, in a World dominated by Beatles, Stones, Vietnam protests and a swathe of folkies.  Hard Rock just getting going, defined by Deep Puple and Sabbath, and some having a go at blending the neo-rock with classical sounds, with variable success.<br /><br />Rarely has anyone deliberately tried to change a world,and succeed as well as this. Pink Floyd were finding their feet, still far from their place in the rock pantheon.<br />Yet, this music was a world away from what Fripp and friends were mostly playing in the weeks and months before...so what kind of epiphany did they have?<br /><br />1969 was a huge year, with many bands trying to break free of a mold, many great musicians trying to redetermine where music could be going. From 18,000km and 43 years away, \\"Woodstock\\" seems to have encapsulated A North American showcase of its brightest, battling the British poular music dominance, at least commercially.<br /><br />So, to continue a quantum cosmological allegory....1969 was the Big Bang, and KC, here, a musical and social condensation around a quantum fluctuation.  Now we can see the musical light having emerged from the prior chaos and opacity.<br /><br />In my view (and I'm rarely wrong), \\"In the court..... \\" defined what would be later regarded as \\"Prog\\"<br />The soundscape is indubitably Jazz-Rock Fusion, the sensibilities very jazzy, and the result is a sublime blend indeed.<br /><br />Sinfield's lyrics are genuinely poetic, at times apocalyptic, at others hopeful and lyrical.<br /><br />The rythmic heart, always a hallmark, (if a sometimes opaque aspect) of KC; here Michael Giles and Greg Lake sparkle and drive the show in the up-tempo songs.<br />Lake's vocals were never as good as on this set...strong, heartfelt with admirable control.<br />The keyboards and wind, courtesy Ian McDonald are as well balanced and appropriate as ever.<br /><br />And Fripp's guitar, tinkling or wailing or noodling away. The guys had no problems with form, in this set. Truly inspired.<br /><br />The opener,\\"21st century Schizoid man\\", screams and batters in its bleakness...raucous, harsh, but sets a tone for this and later works.<br />Then, \\"I talk to the wind\\", a most melodic,lyrical, poetic, gentle work, and a welcome respite.<br />Thence to \\"Epitaph\\"...amongst my favorite 3 KC songs..all there...drama, power, poetry, meaning, honest hard work. Few have been able to better this song in the Rock traditions.<br /><br />\\"Moonchild\\", generally criticized for its wandering into nothingness. I love it, and was disturbed at the truncated version first heard on this collection, though not surprized, as I'd read of this previously.<br />Thankfully, the full version is heard as a bonus track.<br />The last, title track is rather like \\"Epitaph\\", but without quite the impact. I forgive them.<br />The first CD contains the full version of Moonchild, and a fascinating duo version of \\"I talk to the wind\\", then an alternative mix; then the backing track only of \\"Epitaph\\", which is illuminating, but lacks the drama...Lake's singing is clearly pivotal.<br /><br />Fripp explains how they came to produce the album in the helpful notes.<br /><br />The 1st disc is essentially Steven Wilson's 2009 make-over , with the shortened Moonchild. The sound quality is brilliant, and better than I remember my Vinyl to be.<br/>The second disc is the 2004 Original master Edition. I don't think the bonus tracks on disc 2 add any more to our grocking of this seminal work.<br /><br />So, boys and girls...(us old geezers' grandchildren)..give up on \\"The Voice\\",\\"X Factor\\", and most of the tripe that the modern iterations of major recording companies think you must enjoy and buy.<br />Leave your MP3s alone.<br />Embrace your inner \\"Proghead\\"<br />Start where it began.<br />Grock.

0393327345|5.0|I'm enjoying a quiet week of reading down at the Outer Banks in North Carolina.  Fight club has been far from quiet.  It's now 2012, 16 years since Chuck Palahniuk published Fight Club, his first novel.  The phenomenon of Fight Club has mostly played out...ever since the movie version propelled this work to household status. Fight Club is fiction.  It's anarchy.  It's schizophrenic.  It's dark.  It's gross.  It's freeing.  It's troubled.  It's poetic.  It's haunting.  It's prophetic.  It's eluring.  It's dangerous.  It's completely wrong.  It's completely right.  I can't think of a better way to spend time reading than bending the damp pages of this book in the safe cocoon of a beach house with the surf in the background and a cold beer within reach, wondering if there's a fight club near me...not that I would go.<br /><br />Palahniuk didn't write this book to be anything but pure entertainment...whatever label fans of this book (and movie) have given it over the years is a testament to the effect it has on it's audience.  At it's very core it is a love story as tragic as any love story ever written.  On the surface it's a manifesto for the rebellion of the common man.  It fails as a love story because you are not moved to tears at its conclusion.  It fails as a revolutionary text because the recipe for rebellion it describes is completely inconsistent and a one way ticket to homelessness, prison, disfigurement, and/or death...not sacrifices one would readily make for a cause without understanding the cause.<br /><br />It achieves, however, a psychological pathology that turns Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde on it's head.  All of us share a common attribute with regard to our brains...we all have a left side and a right side.  In theory the level at which we are all schizophrenic is directly related to how closely the two sides of our brain communicate.  In Julian Jayne's monumental, \\"The Origin of Consciousness in the Breakdown of the Bicameral Mind\\" one could attribute the residual evidence of split personality disorder as a leftover artifact of our brain's evolution...something all of us might posses.  This could mean there's a Tyler Durden hiding in all of us...regardless of whether our personal Tyler Durden looks like Brad Pitt. Inside each of us could be an Oracle, or a madman.  I believe it's this hidden side, the side from which Palahniuk blows us out of the water, that makes his novel, and this work, so important to us fans. B003Y5H4Y8|2.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />'The Debt' is based on a 2007 Israeli film of the same name. The basic plot is the same: three Mossad agents, two men and a woman, attempt to kidnap a Nazi war criminal in East Berlin in 1965 and ferret him back to Israel for trial.<br /><br />Of course the main idea is taken from the actual abduction of Eichmann in Argentina in 1960. There are two main differences between the actual historical event and what we see here. First of all, and most important, the Mossad operation to abduct Eichmann, albeit extremely difficult, was possible as the agents were operating in a relatively free country (as compared to the countries of the Soviet bloc). To accept the idea that the Mossad would even consider attempting to ferret a Nazi war criminal out of East Germany at the height of the Cold War, that's something I had a hard time believing. Even the Mossad, I believe, would have considered such a plan as 'too risky'.<br /><br />The second principal difference between the Eichmann abduction and the one in 'The Debt', is that the fictional Nazi war criminal the Mossad agents abduct, 'Dieter Vogel', is modeled on the sadistic doctor of Auschwitz, Josef Mengele, and not the banal bureaucrat, Eichmann. Like the Vogel character, Mengele posed as a gynecologist, performing illegal abortions after the war. The real-life Eichmann proves much more interesting than the fictional Vogel, pretty much a standard Nazi film villain. Eichmann, on the other hand, is fascinating since he hardly fits the mold of the typical sadistic Nazi--rather, he resembled the ordinary German you might casually bump into randomly on the street--an individual one would hardly suspect as the chief facilitator of the Final Solution.<br /><br />If you think about it, it didn't take a whole lot of imagination on the screenwriters' part to come up with the main plot, which involves a true-life, successful intelligence operation, turned on its head. 'The Debt' in effect is an Eichmann-like abduction gone bad. There is a historical precedent for a botched Mossad operation--the Lillehammer affair, where the Mossad assassinated the wrong man in their quest to eliminate one of the leaders of the Munich massacre. That's a more likely scenario than the unlikely events depicted here. While the botched escape at the train station hardly seemed true to life, at least it was suspenseful. But when Vogel manages to untie himself and escape the apartment, the entire scenario seemed a little too convenient. Worse is the awkward denouement, where Helen Mirren playing the middle-aged heroine Rachel Singer, travels to Ukraine and ends up getting herself done in by the Nazi antagonist, only to dispatch him at the last second (and in the nick of time).<br /><br />It's not that I disliked the whole idea of Singer paying off her debt to her country--it's just that I couldn't believe an aging, retired Mossad agent, would take it upon herself to 'finish the job' herself. It's good for the movies but even retired Mossad agents realize there are certain protocols involved in various intelligence missions and one doesn't 'go rogue', especially when one is pushing seventy.<br /><br />'The Debt' is generally well-acted but in the end, I just didn't care about the love triangle which forms the basis of a rather tedious sub-plot. There was also a lack of continuity between the actors who were playing the younger roles and the older ones depicted in the present day.<br /><br />Perhaps what the film scenarists were getting at is that actors in organizations such as the Mossad, which is generally thought of as pretty favorable in the west, are also fallible. The protagonist certainly redeems herself in the end by sacrificing her life but do we really care? The absurd romantic angle grows tedious and we never become truly invested in the antagonist, who lacks a back story and is more a caricature, than a fleshed out human being.ffect is an Eichmann-like abduction gone bad.
